July 11, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fleta Christina Sabra
Accredited Representative
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated January 9, 2018, the Board upheld an Immigration Judge’s decision denying in discretion the respondent’s application for a fraud waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(H). The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen these proceedings. To the extent that he challenges our last decision, his motion is also in the nature of a timely motion to reconsider. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991) (providing that the nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard). The respondent has also filed a request for a stay of removal, which we denied in an order issued on June 26, 2018.1 The motions to reopen and reconsider, which remain pending and are opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be denied.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent argues that the Board and the Immigration Judge overlooked evidence pertaining to conditions in his native country of Yemen that would be adverse to his family, medical evidence of one of his United States citizen children, and other factors relevant to discretion (Motion to Reopen at 8-15, 36-44). He also accuses the Immigration Judge of bias by “t[aking] over much of the questioning,” and claims that the Immigration Judge unfairly “orchestrated the consequence where [his] and his ex-wife’s cases would be heard [together].” (Id at 19, 51).2
 
The respondent further argues that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of former counsel, and that the Board erred in finding that he failed to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for raising such a claim on his direct appeal (Motion to Reopen at 29-36, 52-55). He accuses former counsel of (1) committing an ethical violation by discussing his case “ex parte” with the Immigration Judge and DHS counsel outside of his presence; (2) coaching him to lie at his hearing and then ridiculing him to the Immigration Judge; (3) failing to submit evidence pertaining to one of his United States citizen child’s Attention Deficit Disorder (ADD); and (4) refusing to make the respondent’s criminal attorney accountable for allowing him to plead guilty to a crime that, unbeknownst to him, would have adverse immigration consequences (Id. at 15-25, 27-39).
 
Ultimately, the respondent wishes to seek reopening to apply for cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(a), as well as protection under the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18.3
 
*2 To the extent the respondent seeks reconsideration of our last decision, he has failed to identify any error of fact or law in our decision that would warrant its reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 56-58 (BIA 2006). We are satisfied that the Immigration Judge and the Board fully and properly reviewed the respondent’s case under the applicable legal precedents. While we acknowledged his numerous positive equities in our last decision, we remain persuaded that the respondent’s significant adverse factors - namely, his numerous false statements to the DHS and to the Immigration Court to obtain immigration benefits over a 15-year period, outweigh his positive equities (Board decision dated Jan. 9, 2018, at 2-3). The respondent does not merit discretionary relief.
 
Moreover, upon review of the record, we are unpersuaded that the Immigration Judge was biased against the respondent or otherwise conducted the hearing in a manner that did not comport with due process. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1) (giving immigration judges authority to ““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien and any witnesses”); Rusu v. INS, 296 F.3d 316, 321-22 (4th Cir. 2002) (providing that alien must be ““accorded an opportunity to be heard at a meaningful time and in a meaningful manner, i.e., ... [to] receive a full and fair hearing on [her] claims”). The Immigration Judge’s conduct during the hearing fell short of displaying a ““deep-seated favoritism or antagonism that would make fair judgment impossible [.]” Liteky v. United States, 510 U.S. 540, 555 (1994).
 
We are also unpersuaded that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. To prevail on such a claim, in addition to complying with certain procedural requirements, an alien must demonstrate that he was prejudiced as a result of his counsel’s ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640 (holding that alien must also show that she was prejudiced by counsel’s actions). The respondent has made no such showing here.
 
Initially, we are unpersuaded that the respondent’s former counsel committed any ethical violations by discussing the case with the Immigration Judge, in DHS counsel’s presence, at the conclusion of the proceedings. As an officer of the court, the respondent’s former counsel would have been subject to discipline for knowingly and with reckless disregard offering false evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.102(c). The respondent continued to perjure himself throughout these proceedings, after the original fraud he committed before the DHS during his adjustment and naturalization processes (I.J. at 18; Tr. at 29.58. 222). Apparently, he perjured himself without former counsel’s urging or knowledge that he would do so (See Motion to Reopen. Attached E-Mail from Patrick Taurel dated Dec. 8. 2016. in which Taurel responded to the allegations against him). Once the respondent admitted to his continued perjury, we find it not only reasonable, but also incumbent upon former counsel, to have notified the Immigration Judge that he was unaware that his client was making false statements during his testimony. It is also not uncommon for an Immigration Judge to discuss an alien’s case with his counsel, along with DHS counsel, outside of the respondent’s physical presence.
 
*3 We also do not find that the respondent was prejudiced by former counsel’s failure to offer evidence of his child’s ADD. There is no indication that this evidence would likely have affected the outcome of these proceedings. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464. 473 (BIA 1992).
 
Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s alleged failure to confront the respondent’s criminal counsel about any substantive or procedural defects of his plea in his criminal case. There is no indication that former counsel in these proceedings was retained for that purpose or agreed to assist the respondent in that regard. The respondent has also admitted in these proceedings that he committed the immigration fraud to which he ultimately pled guilty in his criminal proceedings.
 
As a final matter, we decline to reopen these proceedings to permit the respondent to pursue cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents or protection under the Convention Against Torture. The respondent has not shown his prima facie eligibility for either form of relief, such that reopening of these proceedings would be warranted.
 
Because the respondent committed fraud to procure adjustment of status, he is not an alien lawfully admitted for permanent residence for cancellation purposes. See 240A(a)(1) of the Act; see also Matter of Koloamatangi, 23 I&N Dec. 548, 551 (BIA 2003) (holding that an alien was ineligible for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents because he was “deemed, ab initio, never to have obtained lawful permanent resident status once his original ineligibility therefor [was] determined in [removal] proceedings”D’).
 
The respondent has also failed to make a prima facie showing that he will more likely than not face harm amounting to torture by or with the consent or acquiescence (including willful blindness) of a public official or other person acting in an official capacity, in the Yemeni government upon his repatriation. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18. The respondent generally alleges that he will be tortured in Yemen because his brother and cousin were killed by Houthi rebels after the respondent informed United States immigration officials that Houthi rebels were attending his mosque (Petition For Convention Against Torture filed Feb. 9, 2018, Attached Form I-589 & Accompanying Affidavit). The respondent’s generalized and conclusory statements in this regard are insufficient, without more, to make out a prima facie showing that he is eligible for protection under the Convention Against Torture. We acknowledge the difficult country conditions in Yemen, but evidence of adverse country conditions, without more, is insufficient to show that the respondent himself may more likely than not be tortured upon his repatriation. See generally 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18.
 
*4 In view of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

However, the Board and the Immigration Judges have limited jurisdiction and can grant only those forms of relief from removal that are expressly authorized by Congress. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988). We do not have any authority to instruct the DHS how to operate their detention facilities. To the extent that the respondent may be seeking a bond determination, we note that bond proceedings are separate and apart from removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d). ** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AIWU CHEN
File: AXXX XX0 428 - Pompano Beach, FL
July 10, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Armineh Ebrahimian, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evagelia Stella Solomos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s February 7, 2018, decision denying his motion to continue and deeming his application for asylum abandoned and requests that the record be remanded to the Immigration Judge to file his application for asylum. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly deemed the respondent’s claim for asylum abandoned (IJ at 3-4). See Makir-Marwil v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 681 F.3d 1227, 1235 (11th Cir. 2012) (“If an application for relief is not filed by the deadline set by the IJ, that ground for relief is deemed waived.”); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). The Immigration Judge adequately informed the respondent of the opportunity to file for asylum and the consequences of failure to timely file the application (IJ at 3-5; Tr. at 3-4, 7). As further discussed below, the respondent did not establish a persuasive basis for not timely filing his application.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance. An Immigration Judge “may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. The respondent sought a continuance to find a new attorney (Tr. at 8-9). The Immigration Judge noted that the Department of Homeland Security opposed a continuance (IJ at 5). Despite the respondent’s counsel’s failure to appear for the hearing, the respondent, through a Mandarin interpreter, was advised of the consequences of not filing his asylum application by February 7, 2018, and confirmed that he understood (Tr. at 3-4, 7).1 The respondent was also served with a hearing notice advising him of the same (IJ at 3-4; Jan. 10, 2018, Hearing Notice). The respondent appeared at his February 7, 2018, hearing without an application for relief. While the respondent claims he was unaware of his former counsel’s motion to withdraw, he indicated that he was looking for another attorney, and the Immigration Judge noted that a letter from his sponsor indicated the same (IJ at 4; Tr. at 7-8). Moreover, the respondent acknowledged his understanding of the consequences for failure to file an asylum application by February 7, 2018 (IJ at 3-5; Tr. at 3-4, 7). Under these circumstances, the respondent had not established that “good cause” existed to further continue these removal proceedings (Id). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that a continuance should not be granted where it is being sought “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”); see also Matter of Castro-Turn, 27 I&N Dec. 271, 287 (A.G. 2018) (noting regulatory authorization for continuances).
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the continuance denial, as he has not shown that he is prima facie eligible for asylum, and has not submitted an application for asylum on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation). Therefore, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny him a continuance was in error or that he experienced prejudice as a result. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983) (noting that an alien must show that the denial of the continuance caused actual prejudice).
 
Likewise, the respondent’s request for a remand to apply for asylum is denied. Despite his claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, the respondent does not persuasively explain why he could not timely filed his application before the Immigration Judge. Moreover, as noted above, he has not supported his request for a remand with his asylum application, and thus, he has not established prima facie eligibility for asylum. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988); Najjar v. Ashcroft, 257 F.3d 1262, 1303 (11th Cir. 2001); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Therefore, we are unable to conclude whether any application for asylum is reasonably likely to succeed on the merits. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 357 (BIA 1998). Thus, the record will not be remanded.
 
Finally, to the extent the respondent asserts that his due process rights were violated in the proceedings below, we are unpersuaded. The respondent did not have a constitutionally protected liberty interest in the admission of his application for asylum after the court-ordered deadline had run. See Tang v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 578 F.3d 1270, 1276 (11th Cir. 2009). Further, the respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice such that the outcome of these proceedings may have been affected by the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

1

We do not address the merits of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel because the respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1221-22 (11th Cir. 2003). We note that, despite the respondent’s claim of not receiving the Immigration Judge’s decision, the record shows that the respondent was served with the Immigration Judge’s summary order deeming the asylum application abandoned and ordering him removed to China, as well as being informed of his right to appeal the decision (Tr. at 10-12).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZOILA YOJANA MARTINEZ-ZELAYA
File: AXXX XX0 313 - Dallas, TX
June 28, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 4, 2017, denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on May 12, 2014. On August 11, 2014, the respondent through her former counsel, Mr. Mario Caballero, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging that she did not receive notice of her May 12, 2014, hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion in a decision dated November 28, 2014, finding that the respondent’s failure to appear at the May 12, 2014, hearing was due to her failure to provide an updated address with the Immigration Court. On June 28, 2017, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a second motion to reopen alleging that her new motion was subject to equitable tolling based on exceptional circumstances in the form of Mr. Caballero’s ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion in a decision dated August 4, 2017, finding that while the respondent may have pursued her rights diligently, extraordinary circumstances were not present in this case because the respondent did not meet her burden of proof to show that Mr. Caballero’s representation was ineffective (IJ at 3).
 
On appeal, the respondent specifically contends that her former attorney, Mr. Caballero, provided ineffective assistance of counsel to her because he based the respondent’s first motion on an improper ground, i.e. a lack of notice claim (Respondent’s Br. at 4-5, 9-10). The respondent therefore argues that her second motion, filed more than 90 days after the entry of the final administrative order on May 12, 2014, should be equitably tolled based on extraordinary circumstances in the form of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 7-10).
 
Generally, an alien may file one motion to reopen, which must be filed within 90 days after the entry of the final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Under the doctrine of equitable tolling the 90-day filing deadline can be excused if the respondent establishes that: 1) she has been pursuing her rights diligently, and 2) some extraordinary circumstance stood in her way and prevented a timely filing. Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
*2 Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute extraordinary circumstances. See, e.g., Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that equitable tolling is not warranted in this case because no extraordinary circumstances exist in the form of ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3). The respondent did not fully comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in order to reopen her proceedings based upon her claim of Mr. Caballero’s ineffective representation (IJ at 4-5). Specifically, the respondent’s affidavit did not set forth in detail the representation agreement she had with her former counsel (id.). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639 (requiring a motion to reopen to be “supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel...”) (emphasis added). The documents that the respondent alludes on appeal to do not attest in detail to the facts of the representation agreement at issue and only summarily explain that she retained Mr. Caballero to file a motion to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 11-13; Respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen-Tabs E, J).
 
Furthermore, even if the respondent had met the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding, the respondent must demonstrate “substantial prejudice” resulting from counsel’s performance. Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n. 2 (5th Cir. 2001). Proving prejudice requires that the petitioner make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, she would have been entitled to the relief she sought. Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994). The respondent has not met her burden of showing that she was prejudiced by actions from his prior attorney.
 
Although the respondent contends that her prior attorney did not submit an application for withholding of removal with the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent has not shown that she was prima facie eligible for withholding of removal (Respondent’s Br. at 10). To that end, although the respondent submitted an application for withholding of removal with her Second motion to reopen, the application is blank in a number of places, does not indicate upon what basis she is seeking withholding of removal, and did not include an affidavit from the respondent setting forth the basis of her claim for relief (IJ at 6; Respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen-Tab K).
 
*3 The respondent contends that a reasonable attorney would not have based a motion to reopen on the respondent not having received notice of the May 12, 2014, hearing as it should have been based on exceptional circumstances or on the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen a case sua sponte (Respondent’s Br. at 12). However, the respondent does not advance what those exceptional circumstances would have been. The respondent did not appear for her May 12, 2014, hearing because she did not update her address with the Immigration Court, which led to her not receiving notice of the hearing (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 4, 10)). As the Immigration Judge noted, this does not constitute an exceptional circumstance and the respondent’s motion is untimely (IJ at 3, 5). Further, we agree with the Immigration Judge that based on the totality of the circumstances, the respondent had not presented a truly exceptional situation that would have warranted sua sponte reopening (IJ at 5-6). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
We disagree with the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge conflated his legal analysis regarding equitable tolling with the procedural and substantive requirements of a Lozada claim (Respondent’s Br. at 8). The Immigration Judge separately considered whether the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, and then considered whether the respondent had made out a prima facie case for relief.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Keith E. Hunsucker
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4002303 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 4002316 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIE FRANCINE ELOI
File: AXXX XX8 144 - Miami, FL
June 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Shayne R. Burnham
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Haiti, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of December 4, 2017, denying the respondent’s November 1, 2017, motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on February 9, 2001. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a reply opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s November 1, 2017, motion was the second one filed in these proceedings, it was filed over 16 years after she was ordered removed in absentia, and the Immigration Judge correctly found that the motion was time and numerically barred (IJ at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (providing that there is no deadline for rescinding an order entered in absentia where the issue is notice, and that an alien has 180 days to file a motion where the nonappearance was based on exceptional circumstances).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she is eligible for equitable tolling of the time limits because she was misled by a person posing as a lawyer. However, the respondent has not shown that she complied with the requirements for bringing such a claim, as laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (1988). In particular, nothing in the record indicates that the respondent: (1) filed an affidavit setting forth the agreement that was entered into with this person with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations this individual did or did not make to her in this regard; (2) informed the former attorney or person of the allegations leveled against him/her; or (3) filed a complaint with any appropriate disciplinary authority. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273 & n.6 (11th Cir. 2005) (explaining the Lozada requirements).
 
We find no merit to the respondent’s contention that since it was a non-attorney involved, she need not fulfill the Lozada requirements. See Montano Cisneros, 514 F.3d 1224, 1227 (11th Cir. 2008). Moreover, as the respondent raised the same issue of ineffective assistance of counsel in her October 29, 2001, motion and in his March 13, 2002, decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent had not fulfilled the Lozada requirements, we agree with the DHS that the law of the case doctrine applies to the respondent’s repeated ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See United States v. Amedeo, 487 F.3d 823, 829 (11th Cir. 2007) (courts may readily dispose of repetitive, meritless claims that present the same claim already denied in a prior claim by applying the law of the case where appropriate).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4002316 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 4002320 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YERICIA NICOL ORTIZ-NUNEZ
File: AXXX XX4 227 - Miami, FL
June 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Beresford A. Landers, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angel L. Fleming
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2017, decision denying her motion to reconsider. The respondent sought reconsideration of the Immigration Judge’s October 2, 2017, decision denying her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order dated August 24, 2017. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be sustained.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On December 2, 2016, the respondent, 13 years of age at the time, appeared before the Immigration Judge with present counsel. At the conclusion of that hearing, the Immigration Judge rescheduled the respondent’s case for a later date and waived the respondent’s appearance at future proceedings so that she could attend school (Dec. 2, 2016, Hearing Tr.). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(a). Counsel’s presence was not waived. The respondent’s case was ultimately rescheduled for August 24, 2017 (Mar. 7, 2017, Notice of Hearing). The respondent’s counsel did not appear for this hearing, and the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen on August 29, 2017, claiming that reopening is warranted based on “exceptional circumstances.” Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Although not explicitly framed as such, the exceptional circumstance relied on by the respondent is ineffective assistance of counsel. See Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1226 (11th Cir. 2008) (providing that ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to an exceptional circumstance). The respondent states that the proceedings should be reopened and the removal order rescinded due to counsel’s failure to appear at the hearing (Respondent’s Br.), which counsel claims resulted from unforeseen travel delays on the date of the hearing.
 
In an October 2, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, concluding, without additional explanation, “no exceptional circumstance for the respondent’s failure to appear.” (Emphasis added). On October 19, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider pointing out the fact that the respondent herself was not required to attend the hearing because her presence had been excused.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(a). In an October 29, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider, explaining that “neither the respondent nor counsel were present” at the hearing and that “no exceptional circumstance” had been established. The present appeal ensued.
 
*2 A motion to reopen or reconsider based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires “(1) an affidavit . . . detail[ing] the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken . . ., (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities . . . and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 637 (BIA 1988). The United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has found it reasonable to demand an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel to substantially comply with these procedural requirements. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
The motion to reopen is supported by an affidavit from counsel, detailing the circumstances surrounding his failure to appear at the hearing. Furthermore, counsel’s failure to appear at the hearing, which amounts to deficient performance, is plain on the face of the administrative record. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (“[W]here ‘the facts are plain on the face of the administrative record, the requirements of Lozada are not dispositive.”D’ (citation omitted)), as quoted in Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d at 1222. There is no dispute that counsel missed a hearing he was scheduled to appear at and, in doing so, caused the respondent to be ordered removed in absentia at a hearing she herself was not required to attend. While we share in the dissent’s point that counsel should have self-reported his conduct to relevant bar authorities to satisfy the bar complaint requirement under Matter of Lozada, there is no indication of collusion between the minor respondent and counsel in this case. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996) (noting that the purpose of the bar complaint requirement is to “protect against possible collusion between counsel and the alien client”). On this record, the purpose of Matter of Lozada is sufficiently satisfied.
 
The ineffectiveness of the respondent’s counsel in this case constitutes an exceptional circumstance and was the cause of the respondent’s failure to appear. The respondent, thus, met her burden of establishing that rescission of the in absentia order and reopening of proceedings were warranted.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The August 24, 2017, in absentia order is rescinded, this matter is reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
DISSENTING OPINION: Garry D. Malphrus, Board Member
The issue addressed by the majority--whether counsel’s absence from the hearing constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel--was neither presented to nor considered by the Immigration Judge. As this argument was not advanced below, the proper procedure for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel has not been followed. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 637 (BIA 1988). In particular, there is no indication that relevant bar authorities have been alerted of counsel’s conduct in missing the hearing. See id. (providing that a motion claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must “reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities . . . and if not, why not”).
 
This bar complaint requirement serves the important purpose of “protect[ing] against possible collusion between counsel and the alien client.” Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996). I do not believe, even in jurisdictions that permit only substantial compliance with the requirements under Matter of Lozada, that an attorney should be permitted to bypass this requirement by simply admitting error. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003) (providing that substantial compliance with the requirements under Matter of Lozada is permissible only “where their purpose is fully served by other means”).
 
For these reasons, I respectfully dissent. I would remand the record for the Immigration Judge to address the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel in the first instance under Matter of Lozada.
 
Footnotes

1

The DHS continues to represent on appeal, as it did in its opposition to the motion to reopen, that it “has no record of the respondent’s presence being waived” (DHS’s Br. at 3). The digital audio recording of the December 2, 2016, hearing clearly reflects that the respondent’s presence was waived.

IN RE: MARLON JOSE MORENO-SUNSIN
File: AXXX XX2 645 - Miami, FL
June 20, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irving Joseph Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evagelia Stella Solomos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On January 9, 2008, an Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of removal. On December 6, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. On January 8, 2018, an Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.1
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent argued that he relied upon the advice of someone at his former attorney’s office that he should not appear at the January 2008 hearing (IJ at 2). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had received notice of the hearing, but was arguing that he had failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3 & n.3). The respondent does not challenge this characterization of events on appeal. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not complied with any of the three requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3). On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge relied on the lack of a bar complaint in rejecting his ineffective assistance ot counsel claim (Respondent’s Br.).2
 
However, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not fulfill any of the three requirements, not only the third requirement relating to the filing of a bar complaint. The respondent does not address the other two requirements, i.e., that he provide an affidavit detailing the agreement entered into with counsel and that counsel be informed of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond. As the respondent does not identify any error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not comply with each part of Matter of Lozada, we conclude there is no reason to disturb that portion of the decision. The respondent’s motion is untimely and unexcused. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent emphasizes his desire for sua sponte reopening. In 2013, the respondent married a United States citizen and he argues that he has an exceptional situation in that his wife has documented mental health problems (Respondent’s Br.). Moreover, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have reopened and administratively closed the proceedings so that the respondent could adjust his status through a waiver before the agency (Respondent’s Br.).
 
Administrative closure is no longer appropriate in most circumstances. Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271 (A.G. 2018). Moreover, the couple married after the respondent was in removal proceedings and had an order entered against him, and thus this outcome cannot be said to be an extraordinary circumstance. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). For the above reasons, we decline to exercise our sua sponte authority. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 107 (1988) (“[M]otions to reopen are disfavored in deportation proceedings . . . [because] [t]here is a strong public interest in bringing litigation to a close as promptly” as possible.).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ASHABEN VISHAL PATEL

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joanna Golding, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Luciana Dubuc
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on October 15, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision on July 28, 2014, which denied her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on November 29, 2011. On February 8, 2018, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and reconsider our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely. The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on October 15, 2015. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), with certain exceptions not pertinent here, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion to reopen until February 8, 2018 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen and Reconsider (Motion)). The respondent has not shown that an exceptional situation exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent contends that she filed the instant motion to reopen because she identified substantial issues relating to proper notice and improper representation by prior counsel (Motion). The respondent asserts that she intends to pursue all possible relief and to supplement the motion with evidence of her eligibility for relief warranting reopening in this matter (Id.).
 
On October 15, 2015, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge determination that the respondent received proper notice of her hearing held on November 29, 2011, and that she had neither alleged nor demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel (Board Dec. at 1-2). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s instant motion raises the same issues that we considered and decided in our decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen proceedings following the entry of in absentia order of removal (Id.; Motion). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), (ii).
 
*2 The respondent did not demonstrate any error in our prior decision (Motion). She did not establish that her failure to appear at her scheduled immigration hearing was due to ineffective assistance of counsel or that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s performance (Id.). In her motion, the respondent states that she intends to submit new evidence to support her motion and general contention that she is eligible for relief (Id.). However, she has only submitted one new document, a Form I-797, Notice of Action, dated February 22, 2016, which indicates that she filed a Form I-918 petition for U nonimmigrant status. She has not submitted any other applications for relief. The respondent has not established that she is currently eligible for any relief from removal. The respondent has not established she did not receive proper notice of her hearing in 2011 or that her failure to appear at the hearing was beyond her control. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016) (an alien must establish that he pursued his or her rights with “reasonable diligence” and that his failure to appear was beyond his control); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33, 36 (BIA 1998) (even where the exercise of due diligence is not a statutory requirement for reopening an in absentia deportation order, an alien’s lack of diligence may be a “factor that undercuts” the proffered explanation for the alien’s failure to appear at a scheduled immigration hearing).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: IRVIN JOSIMAR URBINA-MORALES
File: AXXX XX9 928 - Atlanta, GA
June 7, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Rounds, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 20, 2017, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on February 7, 2017. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contention, notice on the respondent’s attorney constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he never received notice of his hearing from his former counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. Here, however, the respondent in his motion to reopen did not allege ineffective assistance by his former counsel. To the extent the respondent now on appeal alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, he has not complied with any of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. Specifically, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit describing the facts and his agreement with his former counsel, nor has he provided any evidence that his former counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before he filed his brief to the Board, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his prior attorney. Consequently, the respondent has shown no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening. The respondent has not explained why his previous counsel was unable to contact the respondent, he has conceded in his motion that he moved to California, and while he alleges he tried to call the Immigration Court, there is no evidence in the record that the respondent filed a written change of address with the Immigration Court.1 Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: YOLVER ESTRADA-MENDOZA YOLBER MENDOZA ESTRADA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas D. Nelson, Esquire
 
AMENDED DECISION
 
*1 ORDER:
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 21, 2017, which found that he had abandoned his opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), but granted his request for voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The record shows that during the September 8, 2016, hearing the Immigration Judge explained to the respondent and counsel that he set a deadline of December 7, 2016, for the filing of the application (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 31). During the next hearing, the Immigration Judge extended the deadline and advised the respondent and counsel that his application must be filed no later than March 17, 2017 (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 39-40). In his June 21 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge observed that no application had been filed and deemed it abandoned (IJ at 1-4). For the reasons provided in the written decision, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination to deem the application abandoned (IJ at 1-4). 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1003.31(c). On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, concedes that the application was not filed with the Court, but explains that “counsel has committed a mistake,” and counsel seeks to take full responsibility for the failure to file (Respondent’s Brief at 5). To the extent the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, we find no “substantial compliance” with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Castillo-Perez v. I.N.S., 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.1
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen, we construe it as a motion to remand because it was filed during the pendency of this appeal. Although the respondent has finally submitted a copy of the application with his motion, the motion is denied. This Board is an appellate body charged with reviewing determinations made by Immigration Judges. We discern no error by the Immigration Judge in deeming the application abandoned. Further, the respondent has not met his heavy burden or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248 and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).

IN RE: ZOLTAN ZEMLENYI

APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Janossy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s December 28, 2017, decision that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2004) (considering an alien’s claim that ineffective assistance of counsel qualifies as an exceptional circumstance warranting rescission of an in absentia order); Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 337 F.3d 892 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that alien established that his failure to appear was due to his reasonable and justifiable reliance on the advice of his attorney’s agent, such that alien was entitled to rescission of in absentia removal order on basis of exceptional circumstances). Upon our de novo review, we conclude that based upon the totality of the circumstances in this case the respondent has met the burden of demonstrating exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear and thereby his November 16, 2017, in absentia order of removal is rescinded. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 3416269 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARLA MARINA QUIJANO-DE VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX9 877 - San Antonio, TX
May 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisette Cardo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Todd Keller
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s December 6, 2017, decision denying her motion to reopen the removal proceedings and to rescind an in absentia removal order issued on March 24, 2006. In conjunction with the appeal the respondent has filed documentary evidence relating to her asylum claim under section 208(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(a)(1)(B), which we construe as a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a motion for summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On November 10, 2005, the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (“NTA”) ordering her to report for a hearing at the Immigration Court in San Antonio, Texas, at a date and time to be set, and she was advised in her native language, Spanish, of her obligation to inform the Immigration Court of her address (IJ at 1; Exh. 1 at 2). The respondent did not report an address. A hearing was scheduled at the San Antonio Immigration Court for March 24, 2006. The respondent did not appear for the hearing on March 24, 2006, and was ordered removed in absentia on that date. On July 22, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and change venue, which was denied. On November 16, 2017, the respondent filed a “Motion to Rescind [the] In Absentia Removal Order and to Reopen Removal Proceedings Pursuant to Sua Sponte Authority.” The respondent sought reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, changed country conditions, and based on the Immigration Judge’s sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings.
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing or was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). Exceptional circumstances include serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. Under certain circumstances, the filing deadline for a motion to reopen may be subject to equitable tolling. See, e.g., Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
*2 As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent did not comply with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). While we acknowledge that those requirements are not in every case mandatory, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish that the attorney whom she asserts rendered ineffective assistance of counsel in fact represented her at the relevant time (IJ at 4). Furthermore, she has not otherwise complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, in particular the requirement that prior counsel be informed of the asserted ineffective assistance. As such, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal that the Immigration Judge should have granted equitable tolling of the time and motion deadlines on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. In any event, such ineffective assistance of counsel would only relate to the first motion, not to the respondent’s failure to appear at her scheduled hearing. We conclude, for the reasons given by the Immigration Judge, that equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted in this case (IJ at 4-5).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish changed country conditions as a basis for reopening. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not submitted information regarding relevant conditions in El Salvador in 2006, and therefore did not provide a meaningful basis for comparison with conditions in 2017, when her most recent motion was filed (IJ at 6). Furthermore, she did not corroborate a significant aspect of the claimed change, namely that gang members were now targeting members of the church to which she belongs, the Episcopal Church (IJ at 6). In view of the foregoing, the respondent has not met her burden to establish a basis for reopening based on changed country conditions pursuant to section 240(C)(7)(c)(ii) of the Act; see generally Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007).
 
Further, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s claim that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not provided evidence of circumstances that would justify the extraordinary remedy of reopening her removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g., Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (holding that discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
*3 The respondent has submitted evidence on appeal. Upon review of the evidence, we decline to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider this evidence, as the evidence has not been shown to affect the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3416269 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 3416267 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIOKAPESI ANAUKIMOANA VILINGIA A.K.A. SIOKAPESI HOLA
File: AXXX XX1 044 - Phoenix, AZ
May 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin T. Wiesinger, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 16, 2017, denying her second motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia on May 5, 2010. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
As an initial matter, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and numerically barred, inasmuch as it was not submitted within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 2010, decision and is the respondent’s second motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b).
 
We are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that the numerical bar should be equitably tolled on the basis that she was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel who filed her prior motion to reopen. Although the respondent complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), she did not establish that her former attorney’s actions were actually prejudicial to her case. See id. at 640; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent was present in Court when the Immigration Judge issued the May 3, 2010, filing deadline for any application for relief and informed the parties of the consequences of failing to meet the deadline. The respondent did not seek or identify voluntary departure as a form of relief, and when she failed to submit an application for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge properly deemed her application abandoned and issued a removal order on May 5, 2010. The respondent did not file her first motion to reopen until nearly seven years later. In the interim, she did not maintain contact with her attorney, assumed that her removal proceedings may have been administratively closed, and failed to inquire with the Court regarding the status of her case. Such actions were not reasonable and do not warrant tolling of the filing deadline for the seven year period until her first motion to reopen was filed in May 2017. Regardless of any deficiencies in the respondent’s first motions, they were untimely filed. The respondent has not demonstrated that any of her motions to reopen should have been equitably tolled, as she has not established that the outcome of her proceedings may have been affected by the ineffective assistance she allegedly received. Thus, the respondent did not establish actual prejudice due to the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005). In light of the foregoing, we conclude that a remand for further consideration of this issue is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3416267 (BIA)
End of Document
