KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Case Reopened by IN RE: SAMRA ORIC, BIA, April 20, 2018
2018 WL 1897710 (BIA)
 
Unpublished
  
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMRA ORIC
File: AXXX XX1 016 - Chicago, IL
February 20, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Katherine M. Dittmeier Holm, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Senat
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Bosnia-Herzegovina, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 5, 2017, decision finding her removable as charged and denying her request for a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent first challenges the Immigration Judge’s finding that she is removable as charged (IJ at 2).1 The record reflects, however, that the respondent’s counsel before the Immigration Judge conceded the charge of removability at her final hearing on August 30, 2017. The respondent initially denied the charge of removability on July 19, 2017 (Tr. at 6). However, at the August 30, 2017, hearing, in response to the Immigration Judge’s inquiry of whether he thought the respondent’s conviction is an aggravated felony for theft, the respondent’s counsel stated that he agreed with the government (Tr. at 13-14). Based on this assertion, we conclude that the respondent conceded the charge of removability before the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent has not established why she should not be bound by her attorney’s concession. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (“Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission.”). Notably, the respondent has not raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim on appeal in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Alternatively, we would affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the DHS established respondent’s conviction under KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030, theft by unlawful taking, qualifies as an aggravated felony theft offense under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G) (IJ at unmarked 2; Tr. at 14).
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was convicted of the offense of theft of a controlled substance and was sentenced to 1 year of incarceration (IJ at 1). The respondent concedes on appeal that subsection (1)(a) of KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030 meets the generic definition of theft (Respondent’s Br. at 10). See Matter of Garcia-Madruga, 24 I&N Dec. 436, 440 (BIA 2008). However, she argues that subsection (1)(b) does not meet the generic definition, rendering the statute overbroad (Respondent’s Br. at 10-12).
 
*2 Assuming arguendo the statute is overbroad, the weight of the evidence points to the conclusion that the statute is divisible. Mathis v. United States, 136 S. Ct. 2243 (2016). Kentucky’s jury instructions for the offense of theft of a controlled substance closely track the language of KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030(1)(a), which the respondent agrees meets the federal definition of a theft (Respondent’s Br. at 10). See 1-6 Cooper & Cetrulo, Kentucky Jury Instructions § 6.18C.2 Further, upon review of the respondent’s conviction record “for the sole and limited purpose of determining whether the listed items are elements of the offense,” the language of the indictment also tracks the statutory language found at KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030(1)(a) (Exh. 2 at 8). See Mathis v. United States, 136 S. Ct. at 2256-57 (internal quotations omitted).
 
The respondent argues that Kentucky case law establishes that KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030 is indivisible (Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). In support of this argument, the respondent cites to Cooley v. Commonwealth, 821 S.W.2d 90, 91 (Ky. 1991), and Commonwealth v. Day, 559 S.W.2d 166 (Ky. 1980). However, the discussion within Cooley v. Commonwealth, 821 S.W.2d at 91, compares the relationship between KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030(1)(a) and KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.110, and the analysis in Commonwealth v. Day, 559 S.W.2d at 166, solely considers subsection (1)(a) of KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030. Neither decision discusses a comparison between subsections (1)(a) and (1)(b) of KY. REV. STAT. ANN. § 514.030.
 
Finally, the respondent contests the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny her request for a continuance (IJ at 2-3; Respondent’s Br. at 13-15). An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance for good cause shown. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. A decision to deny a motion for a continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that the denial caused her actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of her case. Matter of Villarreal-Zuniga, 23 I&N Dec. 886, 891-92 (BIA 2006); see also Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983). Further, when requesting a continuance for the purpose of obtaining and presenting additional evidence, the applicant must support such a request by establishing that “any additional evidence he seeks to present is probative, noncumulative, and significantly favorable.” Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. at 356.
 
*3 The respondent requested a continuance at her August 30, 2017, hearing for the purpose of awaiting resolution of her request in state court to obtain post-conviction relief (IJ at 2-3; Tr. at 14-17). The record reflects that the respondent was previously granted a continuance on July 19, 2017, in order to await the resolution of her request for post-conviction relief and to research the issue of removability (IJ at 2; Tr. at 9-11). The Immigration Judge appropriately denied any further continuance.
 
Further, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s denial of her request for a continuance. As noted above, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent is removable. She has not submitted evidence on appeal reflecting that there have been judicial rulings altering the status of her conviction. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996) (“[T]he Immigration Judge and this Board cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the alien.”).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

After correctly noting the removability charge as reflected in the Notice to Appear, the Immigration Judge stated that the sustained the charge of removability based on a crime involving moral turpitude (IJ at 1-2). This appears to be a typographical error.

2

We note that it makes sense that the jury instructions track the language of subsection (1)(a), as it seems improbable that a controlled substance would be “immovable property.”

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MERCY MUMBURA
File: AXXX XX8 491 - Atlanta, GA
February 13, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eli A. Echols, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated May 9, 2017, which denied her motion to reopen removal proceedings held in absentia. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board) (Lozada). She argued the Immigration Judge erred when he failed to accept the prior counsel’s admission concerning advising the respondent of the wrong date of the removal hearing, 2016 vs. 2017, as substantial compliance with Lozada.
 
A successful claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy certain conditions. First, the claim must comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. Briefly, the Lozada requirements are (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit detailing counsel’s failings, (2) that counsel be informed of the allegations, and (3) that the motion show that disciplinary charges were filed with the appropriate authority. Id.; see also Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003) (finding the alien did not substantially comply with Lozada requirements, and therefore was not entitled to reopen removal proceedings based on claim that he suffered from ineffective assistance of counsel during his appeal to BIA).
 
Here, the respondent did not fulfill the procedural requirements for such claims as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 638-39. The record is devoid of an affidavit from either the respondent or counsel concerning the events or conversations that took place surrounding the scheduled hearing date. We recognize that prior counsel was informed of the issues concerning the in absentia finding by the Court, as he submitted the motion to reopen, which explained that he did not advise the respondent of actual hearing date (Respondent’s Br. at 4). However, the respondent has not made such an assertion, and statements made by counsel in a brief or motion are not considered evidence and, thus, are not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505-6 (BIA 1980) (finding that “[c]ounsel’s arguments are not evidence . . .”).
 
*2 Nevertheless, the respondent’s motion to reopen indicated, “Respondent and Counsel erroneously thought the new Court date was February 25, 2017,” without detailing how the respondent learned about her hearing date (Respondent’s Br. at 4). Further, the respondent did not proffer an affidavit detailing her counsel’s failings at the Immigration Court level or on appeal with the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. In addition, there is no evidence the respondent filed a complaint with the appropriate authority that detailed prior counsel’s insufficient actions in her case. Id; Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d at 1222 (finding that substantial compliance is not found where a respondent does not submit a sworn affidavit attesting to the relevant facts of his ineffective assistance claim, nor has he provided his former counsel with notice and an opportunity to respond). As the respondent did not substantially comply with the Lozada requirements concerning the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision, and will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1897739 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: SANDRA LORENA HERNANDEZ-ORTEZ ABNER FABRICIO MAYEN-HERNANDEZ

AXXX XX3 762

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Michelle N. Mendez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Donald W. Cassidy
Associate Legal Advisor
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 On October 11, 2017, this Board determined that the respondent’s appeal was untimely filed and that the appeal should be dismissed for that reason.1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider and remand. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the motion, and the respondent has filed a reply.2 The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded.
 
The record establishes that the respondent’s appeal was filed over 2 years after the 30-day appeal period set out at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). The Notice of Appeal, however, was accompanied by a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by Christopher Taylor, Esquire. The Notice of Appeal did not include sufficient documentation to show that the respondent had complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). However, on September 20, 2017, the respondent filed an emergency motion to stay removal and additional documents in support of the respondent’s appeal. These documents included the bar complaint filed against Attorney Taylor and a statement by the respondent concerning both her claim of ineffective assistance and an explanation of why she was still working with the attorney and the attorney’s law firm until a short time before she filed the untimely appeal. The respondent asserts, among other things, that the attorney did not file an asylum application on her behalf even though she feared return to El Salvador and that the attorney sought only a removal order, contrary to her understanding of what the attorney had been paid to do. In this regard, the respondent observes that her contract was in Spanish except for the form of relief she would be seeking, which was in English (Mot. to Reconsider, Exh. A at 5-6). She states that the English was not translated or explained to her (Id.). She states that she would not have paid an attorney for a removal order (Id.).
 
It does not appear that the additional documents filed by the respondent on September 20, 2017, were considered by this Board prior to the issuance of our decision on October 11, 2017. Furthermore, the Board’s last decision did not address the previously filed response by the law firm to another complaint, in which there was an apparent admission that the firm policy was that persons from Central American countries generally did not have a valid claim to asylum so the firm did not file asylum applications except in rare cases and instead accepted client fees for the purpose of seeking delays in the client’s removal.
 
*2 Given that the respondent substantially complied with Lozada before our last decision was issued, that decision will be reconsidered.3 Upon reconsideration, we conclude that the respondent has sufficiently established ineffective assistance of counsel. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1272-74 (11th Cir. 2004). Although two attorneys at the law firm representing the respondent wrote letters denying the allegations against them (Emergency Motion to Stay Removal filed Oct. 30, 2017, Exh. K at 53, 57), we note that the respondent has produced documents that call into question the statements in the letters. For example both letters report that the respondent did not show up for her immigration check-in on May 24, 2017, but the respondent produced evidence that she did appear (Mot. to Reconsider Exh. E at 49). The respondent claims that it was the attorneys who did not show up to meet with her at the check-in even though an attorney was supposed to go with her (Mot. to Reconsider, Exh. A at 13).4 Based on the evidence before us, it appears that the respondent sought to apply for asylum and might have qualified for asylum but was not given the chance to present her case due to the ineffective assistance she received.
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent has demonstrated due diligence in pursuing her case such that equitable tolling applies to the filing deadline for the respondent’s appeal. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 717 F.3d 847, 851 (11th Cir. 2013) (holding that an alien is entitled to equitable tolling generally where he shows that he has been pursuing his rights diligently and that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way); Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y. Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1363 (11th Cir. 2013). The respondent explained that her attorneys convinced her that her case was not over and that they were still working to assist her (Filing Dated Sept. 20, 2017, Exh. F).
 
Finally, while the respondents could have filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging ineffective assistance as opposed to an appeal with the Board, the particular circumstances presented by this matter persuade us to accept the respondents’ appeal on certification. We find that a remand is warranted to allow the respondents to have a hearing on the application for asylum and related relief.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated October 11, 2017, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is taken on certification and upon consideration of the appeal, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*3 Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2

We also acknowledge the brief submitted by Amicus Curiae and the reply to that brief filed by the DHS.

IN RE: SEBASTIAN CZYZ

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dana Roxana Bucin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leigh Mapplebeck
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 By an untimely motion received by the Board on August 16, 2017, the respondent seeks to have these proceedings reopened. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on October 27, 2005. By a decision of that date, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the decision of an Immigration Judge of November 29, 2004, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent was barred from obtaining such relief by section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d) (failure to depart after receiving voluntary departure). The respondent had been previously afforded the privilege of voluntary departure by a Stipulated Agreement Requesting Voluntary Departure in Lieu of Removal, signed by the respondent and his legal representative on June 22, 1999. The respondent failed to depart as required.
 
In support of the motion now before the Board, the respondent has failed to submit an application for relief as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Further, the respondent also attempts to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel but has failed to comply with the requirements for asserting such a claim. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, the respondent argues that the proceedings must be reopened and terminated because they were improvidently initiated by the Notice to Appear issued on December 7, 1998. The respondent bases this argument on the fact that he was charged with removability pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), as inadmissible at the time of his adjustment of status pursuant to section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), 8 U.S.C. § 1182 (a)(7)(A)(i)(I). Citing Ortiz-Bouchet v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 714 F.3d 1353 (11th Cir. 2013), the respondent argues that the aforenoted charge was facially defective because the stated ground of deportability is inapplicable to those, like the respondent, who sought post-entry adjustment of status.1 We disagree and further note that the aforenoted case is not controlling in the jurisdiction within which these proceedings arise.
 
*2 The Board considers adjustment of status as a form of “admission,” not only for purposes of determining inadmissibility and removability but also for purposes of establishing eligibility for relief from removal. See Matter of Chavez-Alvarez, 26 I&N Dec. 274, 276 (BIA 2014). Therefore, the charge lodged against the respondent was appropriate and we find no basis to terminate these proceedings.
 
There being no basis for the sua sponte reopening of this matter, or for any exception to the regulatory time limits placed on motions to reopen, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent submitted an application for adjustment of status on March 21, 1997, as a derivative of an application filed on behalf of his father. The respondent’s application was approved in error on January 8, 1998, insofar as the respondent attained the age of 21 August 1, 1997, and did not qualify as a derivative beneficiary. While the respondent now contends that his adjustment of status was valid because he was under 21 when his father’s visa petition was approved, he conceded removal in a stipulation entered in 1999. We will not revisit the question.

2018 WL 1871963 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PERCY DE LA PORTILLO SEMINARIO A.K.A. PERCY SEMINARIO DE LA PORTILLO
File: AXXX XX6 983 - Houston, TX
February 9, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Asa Borts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bianca Bansal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
An Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on July 8, 2002, because he failed to appear for the hearing. The Immigration Judge also held that the respondent had failed to submit a complete motion for change of venue that complied with local rules. On April 11, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance because she failed to submit a complete motion for change of venue and led the respondent to believe that he did not have to appear for his hearing in Texas because the case had been transferred to another Immigration Court. He also alleged in the motion that he is married to a United States citizen and is eligible for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the motion is untimely, that the respondent cannot qualify for an exceptional circumstances exception to the deadline, that he did not allege equitable tolling of the deadline and would not qualify for such tolling because he did not comply with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and did not exercise due diligence in pursuing his rights. In addition, the Immigration Judge found that sua sponte reopening is not warranted (IJ at 2-4).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues the Immigration Judge’s July 8, 2002, order of removal is evidence of his prior attorney’s ineffective assistance because the Immigration Judge noted in the order that the attorney failed to submit a complete motion for change of venue. The respondent also attaches to his appeal new evidence containing an unsworn declaration from his prior attorney, the respondent’s declaration, and evidence obtained from the record of proceedings through a Freedom of Information Act request. The respondent argues that this evidence shows ineffective assistance of his prior attorney and that the Board should reopen and remand.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. First, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely because it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia removal order, and the respondent has not established exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), or lack of proper notice of the hearing. See section 240(b)(5) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 Further, we agree the Immigration Judge that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel could provide a basis for equitable tolling of the 180-day deadline. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 343-44 (5th Cir. 2016); Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798 (5th Cir. 2007). However, the respondent did not allege equitable tolling in his motion to reopen, and he would still have to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d sub nom. Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not comply with the requirements, and we will not consider new evidence on appeal (IJ at 3); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 36-37 (BIA 1995); Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012) (requiring strict compliance with Lozada). Moreover, as the Immigration Judge found the respondent would not qualify for equitable tolling because he waited almost 15 years to file his motion to reopen and did not show that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his rights (IJ at 3); Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d at 344 (alien must show he pursued his rights with reasonable diligence and that some extraordinary circumstances beyond his control prevented him from timely filing).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish an exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening of his removal proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1871963 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
  Opinion Vacated and Superseded by IN RE: YOLVER ESTRADA-MENDOZA YOLBER MENDOZA ESTRADA, BIA, May 31, 2018
2018 WL 3416219 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOLVER ESTRADA-MENDOZA YOLBER MENDOZA ESTRADA
File: AXXX XX9 566 - San Diego, CA
February 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas D. Nelson, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 21, 2017, which found that he had abandoned his opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), but granted his request for voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The record shows that during the September 8, 2016, hearing the Immigration Judge explained to the respondent and counsel that he set a deadline of December 7, 2016, for the filing of the application (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 31). During the next hearing, the Immigration Judge extended the deadline and advised the respondent and counsel that his application must be filed no later than March 17, 2017 (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 39-40). In his June 21 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge observed that no application had been tiled and deemed it abandoned (IJ at 1-4). For the reasons provided in the written decision, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination to deem the application abandoned (IJ at 1-4). 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1003.31(c). On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, concedes that the application was not filed with the Court, but explains that “counsel has committed a mistake,” and counsel seeks to take full responsibility for the failure to file (Respondent’s Brief at 5). To the extent the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, we find no “substantial compliance” with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Castillo-Perez v. I.N.S., 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen, we construe it as a motion to remand because it was filed during the pendency of this appeal. Although the respondent has finally submitted a copy of the application with his motion, the motion is denied. This Board is an appellate body charged with reviewing determinations made by Immigration Judges. We discern no error by the Immigration Judge in deeming the application abandoned. Further, the respondent has not met his heavy burden or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
*2 permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond mat time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent tails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 12S2, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Pauley Roger

2018 WL 1871978 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR EDIBERTO RAMOS-HERRERA
File: AXXX XX3 755 - Atlanta, GA
February 6, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Noemi Puntier, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jack D. Spencer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the September 6, 2017, order of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent was previously ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on June 8, 2017. Section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in determining that he did not establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. The respondent asserts that his failure to appear for the hearing was beyond his control, because during the prior hearing the Spanish court interpreter erroneously translated the date of the scheduled hearing and the respondent’s former counsel did not notify him of the correct hearing date.
 
An order issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “““exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on July 7, 2017. Thus, the respondent’s motion to reopen is timely filed. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). In his affidavit, the respondent stated that the court interpreter stated in Spanish that the scheduled hearing would be on August 8, 2017. The respondent’s former counsel acknowledged in a signed declaration that he properly received the notice of hearing for June 8, 2017, but failed to notify the respondent of the scheduled hearing. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent’s former counsel also did not appear in Immigration Court on June 8, 2017, because of his representation in a juvenile court on that date; he did not realize that he missed the scheduled hearing until he received the Immigration Judge’s order. Finally, the respondent’s former counsel, in his declaration, stated that he listened to the audio recording from the August 4, 2016, hearing and that he heard the interpreter translate the scheduled hearing on June 8, 2017 as “ocho Agosto.”
 
*2 Based on the evidence of record, we conclude that the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. See Dakane v. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2004) (requiring an alien to demonstrate “substantial, if not exact, compliance with the procedural requirements of Lozada”). Moreover, we note that the respondent had an incentive to appear for the hearing, as the respondent received an approval notice dated December 13, 2016, for his Form I-360 petition for Special Immigrant Juveniles.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, the in absentia order of removal is rescinded, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 1871977 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: THANH CONG HUYNH
File: AXXX XX8 810 - Seattle, WA
February 6, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary D. Spencer, Esquire1
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret LaDow
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand; continuance
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Vietnam who appeals from a January 31, 2017, Immigration Judge decision finding him removable as charged2 and denying his request for a continuance. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s most recent request for a motion to continue after concluding that the respondent had not demonstrated good cause for another continuance. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny his motion.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard and we review all other issues de novo, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002).
 
The respondent was admitted to the United States on or about February 27, 2007, as a lawful permanent resident. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred and denied his right to due process by denying his request for a motion to continue when he had a prima facie case for post-conviction relief. He asserts that his first counsel was ineffective because he did not file an application on his behalf.
 
A party seeking a continuance has the burden to establish good cause for a continuance. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 812-14 (BIA 2012); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790-93 (BIA 2009); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355-56 (BIA 1983); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. In determining whether good cause exists to continue proceedings, an Immigration Judge may consider a variety of factors, including (1) the Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) response to the motion; (2) whether the underlying application for relief is prima facie approvable; (3) whether the respondent is statutorily eligible for and deserving of the requested relief; and (4) whether the respondent presents a valid reason for the continuance, and other procedural factors. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. at 790.
 
In the case at bar, the respondent first appeared before an Immigration Judge on September 8, 2015. An interpreter was not available so the proceedings were continued until January 6, 2016. Tr. at 2-4. On January 6, 2016, the Immigration Judge continued the proceedings in order for the respondent to have an opportunity to secure counsel (Tr. at 12). On September 26, 2016, the respondent appeared before the Immigration Judge with counsel and conceded the charge involving a violation of a law relating to a controlled substance (Tr. at 15-17, 20). The respondent’s counsel told the Immigration Judge that he intended to apply for cancellation of removal for certain lawful permanent residents and the Immigration Judge informed counsel that he needed to submit the completed application at the next hearing or it would be deemed abandoned (Tr. at 20-24).
 
*2 On January 31, 2017, the respondent’s counsel told the Immigration Judge that the respondent had retained separate counsel in order to pursue post-conviction relief because his criminal counsel did not provide warnings with regard to the possible immigration consequences of pleading guilty. The respondent’s counsel asked for a six-month continuance in order to complete his motion for post-conviction relief and then move to terminate. Tr. at 30-31; IJ at 2. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s most recent request for a continuance, concluding that he had not demonstrated good cause shown because a pending post-conviction motion generally does not negate the finality of the conviction for immigration purposes. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not filed his post-conviction relief. IJ at 2.
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s most recent request for a continuance. He was granted several continuances over approximately a year and a year in order to hire an attorney and file any applications for relief. At the time of the last hearing below, the respondent indicated that he had hired another attorney in order to pursue post-conviction relief, but had not actually filed any documents with the criminal court. The respondent also did not submit other relevant evidence to his plea agreement or any documents which might support his claim that he had not been advised of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea. Moreover, the respondent plead guilty to and was convicted of attempted violation of the controlled substance act, manufacture of marijuana, in violation of WASH. REV. CODE. ANN. §§ 9A.28.020 and 69.50.41(1)(2)(C), on October 2, 2009 (Exh. 2). There is no indication in the record, nor does the respondent contend, that he appealed that conviction. Thus, it became a final conviction once the 30-day appeal period passed.
 
The Supreme Court held in Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 368-75 (2010), that an alien must be advised of the immigration consequences of a guilty plea. Critically, however, that rule is not retroactively applied, and only pertains to convictions that are final after the March 31, 2010, Padilla decision. Chaidez v. U.S., 568 U.S. 342, 348-355 (US 2013). This respondent’s conviction became final in November 2009, several months before Padilla was issued. Thus, he does not fall under the umbrella of its prospective rule, and the respondent has not demonstrated he is prima facie eligible for post-conviction relief. Id. Therefore, the respondent did not demonstrate good cause for another continuance below, and we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s most recent motion to continue. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. at 790-93. For the foregoing reasons, we determine that the proceedings below were fair to the respondent, and that he has not demonstrated a statutory or regulatory violation.
 
*3 The respondent asserts that his first counsel was ineffective because he did not file an application for cancellation of removal or a brief in support of possible relief. An ineffective assistance of counsel claim must be supported by (1) an affidavit from the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel regarding the matters the attorney was retained to address, (2) documentary evidence that the counsel in question was informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond, and (3) proof that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the alleged ineffective assistance (and if this is not filed, why it was not). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Strict compliance of these steps is not required where the ineffective assistance is “plain on the face of the administrative record.” Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597 (9th Cir. 2004) (internal citation omitted).
 
Here, the respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada nor is the respondent’s former counsel’s purported ineffectiveness plain on the face of the record. The respondent submitted a declaration regarding his experience with his former counsel as well as a copy of a bill indicating partial payment for services rendered by the first counsel (Respondent’s Br. at Exhs. E, AA). The respondent did not provide evidence that the counsel was informed of the allegations against him or that a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Thus, the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada. Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d at 598-99.
 
We also conclude that the record does not show a “clear and obvious” case of ineffective assistance. Rodriguez Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). The Immigration Judge informed the respondent that his criminal conviction likely rendered him ineligible for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 3, 13, 20-22). Thus, it was not obvious ineffective assistance for the respondent’s counsel to attempt to seek post-conviction relief in order to vacate the conviction for immigration purposes. However, this respondent would not actually be eligible for Padilla post-conviction relief because his conviction was final before that case was issued. Chaidez v. U.S., 568 U.S. at 348-355. Further, the respondent has not indicated on appeal that he actually ever sought post-conviction relief with the criminal court on any basis, undermining his argument that he is eligible for it.
 
*4 Accordingly, the following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 1871988 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIR MUSTAFA ALI
File: AXXX XX1 971 - Buffalo, NY
February 1, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Caroline L. Doss, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2017, denying his motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on April 2, 2014.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he did not receive notice of the hearing on April 2, 2014, because his first counsel did not inform him that he was required to appear (Respondent’s Br.). The respondent asserts that his first counsel’s submission of two defective motions demonstrates ineffective assistance without need of further inquiry (Id.). The respondent argues that his second counsel filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel but the motion was not in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (Id.). The respondent also indicated that he intends to apply for a U Visa (Id.).
 
The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, advised of his address obligations, and the Notice of Hearing (NOH) was sent to the address he provided on the Form I-830, Notice to EOIR: Alien Address (IJ at 1; Exh. 1, NOH dated September 19, 2013). The respondent argues that he demonstrated a lack of notice or exceptional circumstances for not attending the scheduled hearing on April 2, 2014 (IJ at 1). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute such an exceptional circumstance if the ineffective assistance results in an alien’s failure to appear at his hearing. See Arts v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance of two prior counsels does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639-40 (BIA 1988). The respondent submitted an affidavit detailing his communications with his prior counsels. However, the respondent did not submit copies of letters sent to the two prior counsels whose performance he deemed deficient. Thus, the respondent’s prior counsels, whose integrity or competence is being impugned, were not given an opportunity to respond to the charges against them. Id. The respondent did not file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding the allegedly deficient representation or explain his failure to do so. See Ruiz-Martinez v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 102, 121 (2d Cir. 2008) (recognizing that, while “slavish adherence” to Matter of Lozada is not required, when an alien does not comply with those requirements in any respect, the ineffective assistance of counsel claim is forfeited). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate that his first counsel prevented the respondent from appearing at the hearing held on April 2, 2014, or that the second counsel provided him ineffective assistance that prejudiced his claim (IJ at 1).
 
*2 We also deny the respondent’s request to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal based on his intent to pursue a U Visa application (Form I-918) with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). The respondent has not provided evidence that he submitted a U visa application to USCIS or that the application was received, such as by a Notice of Action (Form I-797). For the reasons stated above, the respondent has not demonstrated an appropriate basis to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on April 2, 2014.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

On January 5, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s prior motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal entered on April 2, 2014, concluding the his failure to appear was due to his failure to notify the Immigration Court of his change of address. The Immigration Court mailed the Notice of Hearing to the address that the respondent provided and it was returned as not deliverable. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent was advised in the Notice to Appear and in the Form I-830, Notice to EOIR: Alien Address, to notify the Court of any change in his address but he did not do so. The respondent did not appeal.

2018 WL 1871991 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS FELIPE GOMEZ
File: AXXX XX5 877 - Memphis, TN
February 1, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Divyesh Gopal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mahdi J. Abdelaziz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 11, 2017, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for his hearing on June 5, 2017. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i),(ii).
 
The record indicates that notice of the respondent’s June 5, 2017, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s last known address on March 7, 2017 (Exh. 2). The respondent states in an affidavit appended to his motion to reopen that he signed a contract with his attorney on May 12, 2017, in which his attorney “““agreed to send a motion for telephonic hearing” for the June 5, 2017, master calendar. The respondent further stated that his attorney told him he only had to present himself at his office on the day of the hearing because the hearing “““would be by telephone if the Immigration Judge allowed it.” The respondent does not indicate that he had any additional interactions with his attorney to confirm the telephonic hearing. In his motion to reopen, the respondent maintains that he appeared at his attorney’s office on June 5, 2017, for a telephonic hearing; however, his attorney had failed to file a motion for telephonic hearing. The respondent asserts in his appeal brief that his attorney’s office called the Immigration Court on the morning of the hearing and advised a receptionist about the “oversight” and requested a telephonic hearing (See Appeal Brief at 4). The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge did not return his attorney’s telephone call or respond to his request for a telephonic hearing (See Appeal Brief at 4). The respondent asserts that his attorney’s office called the Immigration Court in the afternoon and was informed that the Immigration Judge had ordered the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent suggests that he received ineffective assistance from his counsel. The respondent continues to be represented by the same attorney.
 
An order of removal issued after in absentia proceedings may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). Section 240(e)(1) of the Act limits “exceptional circumstances” to situations involving “compelling circumstances” which are “beyond the control of the alien” such as “serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien.” We have also held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 24 I&N Dec. at 27; see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not complied with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 725 (6th Cir. 2007); Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. at 599. As observed by the Immigration Judge, the respondent briefly details in his affidavit the agreement that he had with his attorney, but the record does not contain a copy of the agreement. Also, there is no evidence that a complaint had been filed with the proper disciplinary authority nor has the respondent provided an explanation as to why a complaint should not be filed with the proper disciplinary authority against his attorney (IJ at 3). The alien has the burden to prove exceptional circumstances. See Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 411 (6th Cir. 2003). The respondent has not done so in this case.
 
We also observe that the respondent had been provided with notice of the June 5, 2017, hearing. The respondent does not indicate that he called his attorney’s office to confirm the Immigration Judge’s allowance of the telephonic hearing. A mistaken belief as to whether the hearing was going to be held telephonically does not rise to the level of being an exceptional circumstance. See Acquaah v. Holder, 589 F.3d 332, 336 (6th Cir. 2009) (court held that a mistaken belief as to the correct date of the hearing does not qualify as an exceptional circumstance); Denko v. INS, 351 F.3d 717, 723 (6th Cir. 2003); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) (holding that the Board applies a totality of the circumstances test for determining whether “““exceptional circumstances” prevented an alien’s appearance at a hearing). For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1871991 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHEIKH SECK A.K.A. CHEZKH SECK
File: AXXX XX6 584 - El Paso, TX
January 30, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reinstatement of appeal
 
*1 The record before us shows that the respondent was ordered removed from the United States to Senegal on March 19, 2007. The Immigration Judge’s order reflects that the respondent waived his right to appeal the decision. On March 26, 2007, the respondent filed a pro se appeal. On April 2, 2007, the respondent filed a letter stating that he had changed his mind, wanted “[n]o more appeal,” wanted to go to Africa, and asked the Board to deny the appeal. The Board issued a decision on April 12, 2007, stating that the respondent had withdrawn his appeal and that the record was being returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
On December 6, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reinstate his appeal. The motion argues that the respondent did not actually request withdrawal of appeal in his letter but asked that his appeal be denied and further that the letter indicates an incorrect belief that the respondent could challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision from Senegal. The respondent argues that he did not write the letter but that he found someone in the detention facility who advised him and who wrote the letter. The respondent’s motion further presents several arguments as to why he should not have been found subject to removal and that even if he were subject to removal, he qualified for relief that he had no opportunity to pursue.
 
Insofar as the respondent’s motion is a motion to reopen or a motion to reconsider for the purpose of reinstatement of the appeal, it is untimely. Motions to reconsider must be filed not later than 30 days after the date of the order from which reconsideration is sought. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent filed his motion more than 10 years after the Board’s last decision. Moreover, the respondent does not argue that his motion falls within any statutory or regulatory exception to the filing requirements. Nor does the respondent claim that equitable tolling applies to his motion. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
Assuming the respondent is attempting to set out a case of ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge, he has not demonstrated compliance with the technical requirements for ineffective assistance claims as set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and (as noted previously), has not argued that equitable tolling applies.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated that proceedings should be reopened or reconsidered sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The record shows that appeal was waived in this matter. Insofar as the respondent did appeal, he has not established that the Board incorrectly deemed his appeal withdrawn. The respondent’s letter to the Board in 2007 specifically said “No more appeal” and that the respondent wanted to go to Africa.
 
Moreover, more than 10 years have passed since the Board’s last order. The record does not show that the respondent has challenged that order until now. The respondent’s motion provides no information concerning what has taken place since the Board issued its order. We have no statements about where the respondent is living, whether he was removed to Senegal, or whether he attempted to find alternative counsel to assist him with his immigration issues. We conclude that the respondent has not sufficiently established an exceptional situation that would cause us to sua sponte reopen or reconsider and then reinstate the appeal. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (explaining that sua sponte reopening is reserved for exceptional situations).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1872002 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AVAT SOSA-CACHO
File: AXXX XX3 168 - Cleveland, OH
January 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy M. Crossin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
*1 On September 20, 2013, we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s November 21, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s motion to administratively close his removal proceedings. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status based on an approved Form I-130 petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen mother and a current priority date. The record before us does not contain a brief in opposition from the Department of Homeland Security. The motion to reopen and terminate will be denied.1
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and will be denied as such. With certain exceptions not pertinent here, an alien is entitled to file one motion to reopen and the motion to reopen must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on November 17, 2017, over 4 years after the final order of removal was entered, and the record before us does not present any exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening (see Motion to Reopen, Exhs. 1-25). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to sua sponte reopen proceedings is reserved for “““exceptional situations” and “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.”).
 
We acknowledge that the respondent has submitted numerous documents and statements in support of his motion, including documents regarding his ties to the United States and community, his character, and the potential hardship to his family, including his United States citizen children (see Motion to Reopen, Exhs. 2-15). However, we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, to the extent the respondent is alleging that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance (Motion to Reopen, Exh. 21), he has neither complied with the procedural requirements for such a claim nor submitted evidence to justify equitable tolling. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural and substantive requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F3d 725, 727 (6th Cir. 2007) (stating that an alien who does not comply with the Lozada requirements forfeits his ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 417, 422 (6th Cir. 2007) (stating that equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires a showing of due diligence). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s untimely motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

On November 21, 2017, the Board denied the respondent’s request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of this motion.

2018 WL 1756894 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PETER NDUNGU CHEGE A.K.A. PETER NDUNGU
File: AXXX XX0 439 - Boston, MA
January 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Musa P. Sebadduka, Esquire
 
APPLICATIONS: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was last before us on December 31, 2015, when we entered a final administrative order dismissing the respondent’s appeal. On July 14, 2017, the respondent submitted the instant motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. He requests a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion has been filed out of time, and it will be denied. The respondent’s stay request will also be denied.
 
With limited exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In certain circumstances, an alien may toll the time limit applicable to his motion to reopen by demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In addition to complying with the procedural requirements for such a claim, the alien must show prejudice as a result of the ineffective assistance and that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to toll. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. He seeks reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent claims that when the Immigration Judge asked if he was going to request voluntary departure and his counsel said no, he asked his counsel what the Immigration Judge meant by voluntary departure, and his counsel replied that he would explain it after the hearing. See Exh. VIII, ¶ 10. He relates that his counsel later explained what voluntary departure meant, and stated that he did not need voluntary departure because the Immigration Judge erred and they were going to appeal his decision. Id., ¶ 11. He recounts that after his appeal was dismissed, his counsel advised him to seek review in the Circuit Court of Appeal, and said that if the Circuit Court remands his case to the Immigration Judge, he could apply for voluntary departure. Id., ¶ 13. The respondent states that he was confused and upset because his counsel was telling him that he needed to appeal one more time to achieve what the Immigration Judge had offered him at the start. Id. He reports that he agreed to pursue further appeals to the Circuit Court as a last alternative, and that on May 15, 2017, the Circuit Court affirmed the decision of the Board. Id., ¶ 15. The respondent claims that his counsel should not have appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision because he knew that he was inadmissible and barred from adjustment of status, and that he misrepresented the general outlook of his case, giving him false hope. Id., ¶¶ 16-17. He alleges that his counsel’s false representations cost him his only available option and several thousand dollars in legal fees and court costs that were not necessary, and violated the rules of professional conduct. Id., ¶¶ 18-20.
 
*2 The respondent offers his affidavit and his statement of facts, his notice to his former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance, his counsel’s response, the bar complaint he filed against his former counsel and receipt notice, a Massachusetts record of criminal offender information, and letters from a friend and a parish vicar in the United States.
 
The respondent has not offered persuasive evidence for his claim that his counsel made false representations to him. The record reflects that the respondent agreed to proceed with the appeal and federal court review of his removal proceedings after the Immigration Judge rendered his decision in 2014. We are not convinced by the respondent’s contention at this time, years later and after his appeal has been dismissed and petition for review denied, that his counsel provided ineffective assistance by pursuing those courses of action. We conclude that, although the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not demonstrated that he suffered ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639; Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 12 (1st Cir. 2004); Saakian v. INS, 252 F.3d 21, 25 (1st Cir. 2001). We are not persuaded that equitable tolling of the time limit for motions to reopen is warranted in this case. See Bead v. Holder, 703 F.3d 591, 594 (1st Cir. 2013); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. at 553.
 
The motion to reopen has been filed out of time, and has not been shown to come within any exception to the timely filing requirements for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent has not met his heavy burden to demonstrate that his removal proceedings should be reopened. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). He has not shown the exceptional circumstances necessary to warrant our granting this untimely motion in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, as the motion exceeds the time limit for motions to reopen, it will be denied, and the respondent’s stay request will also be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and stay request are denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1756894 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JENNIFA JOSHUE A.K.A. JENNIFER JOSHUA
File: AXXX XX0 562 - Danbury, CT
January 4, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul B. Grotas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on January 9, 2009, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely filed. On July 11, 2017, the instant untimely, number-barred motion to reopen was filed by the respondent. The motion will be denied.
 
Under the law, with certain exceptions not pertinent here, a party is limited to one motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). As this is the respondent’s second motion to reopen, it is numerically barred. The motion to reopen has also been filed more than 90 days after our decision dismissing the appeal on January 21, 2003, that resulted in a final administrative order of removal. Accordingly, as with the respondent’s previous motion, the instant motion to reopen is untimely.
 
We do not find that there are exceptional circumstances in this case that warrant reopening under our limited sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).1 Moreover, an alien’s potential eligibility for relief after the entry of a final administrative removal order is not, in and of itself, an exceptional circumstance. This is a common situation for many aliens. The respondent asserts, however, that because she was the victim of “fraud and deceit” by someone who was not an attorney, she has significant equities, and her removal would be an extreme hardship on her United States citizen husband and children, her proceedings should be sua sponte reopened (Respondent’s Motion at 14-15).
 
The respondent alleges that she paid and worked closely with an individual, Mr. Victor Espinal, who was not, in fact, an attorney. Mr. Espinal was convicted in 2009, of fraudulently representing himself as an attorney (Respondent’s Motion at 7). Despite the seriousness of these circumstances, the record indicates that Mr. Espinal did not represent the respondent in her removal proceedings; Mr. Espinal never filed a notice of appearance, Form EOIR-28, before the Immigration Court or a Form EOIR-27 before the Board on behalf of the respondent (Respondent’s Motion at 6; Exhs. D, E, M, P).2 Given the lack of representation by Mr. Espinal, we find no basis for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against him.
 
With regard to the allegations made against Mr. Rojas, the respondent has not sufficiently met all of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. We find the respondent’s affidavit to be inadequate as it fails to provide adequate details about the nature of the agreement between the respondent and Mr. Rojas, and the agreed upon scope of their representation. See Piranej v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 137 (2d Cir. 2008) (remanding for further fact-finding in the context of a general retainer agreement). We have considered the other evidence submitted by the respondent, including the payment receipts and correspondence, but conclude that the respondent has not met the requirements of Lozada. Moreover, the respondent has not shown prejudice as a result of any alleged ineffective assistance, as she has not shown that the outcome of her case would likely have been different but for the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 We turn next to the respondent’s contention that the Second Circuit’s decision in Husic v. Holder, 776 F.3d 59 (2d Cir. 2015), constitutes a fundamental change in the law which renders her eligible for a discretionary waiver under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h) (Respondent’s Motion at 12). We acknowledge the change in the law. See also Matter of J-H-J, 26 I&N Dec. 563 (BIA 2015) (holding that section 212(h) of the Act only precludes aliens who entered the United States as lawful permanent residents from establishing eligibility for a waiver on the basis of an aggravated felony conviction).
 
Notwithstanding the change in law, the respondent is not currently the beneficiary of an approved Form I-130 (Petition for Alien Relative), so as to be prima facie eligible for adjustment of status, even if granted a section 212(h) waiver. In addition, “given the evidence of record regarding the respondent’s conviction under counts 1 and 5 of the relevant superseding indictment (Exh. 3),” we remain unconvinced that the waiver (or adjustment of status) would or should be granted in the exercise of discretion. See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals dated January 9, 2009.
 
For all of the foregoing reasons, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

Mr. Espinal never represented the respondent in her claims before the U.S. District Court for the Eastern District of New York (Exh. H), or before the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit (Exh. J).
