** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS ALBERTO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX3 311 - New York, NY
December 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Lopez-Ruiz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on September 22, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision finding his application for adjustment of status abandoned and alternatively finding the respondent statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen urging that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. He also requests a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay will be denied.
 
Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. Romero v. INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent urges that the attorney who represented him at his hearing below was ineffective for failing to timely file his application for adjustment of status, which resulted in it being deemed abandoned. However, the Immigration Judge and the Board alternatively denied the application on its merits. Specifically, we found that the respondent did not establish the requisite parent child relationship for purposes of adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act as a derivative grandfathered alien. The respondent has not submitted any new or additional evidence to establish that he once qualified as his father’s child as defined under section 101(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(b)(1). Therefore, the respondent still has not established eligibility for adjustment of status. As such, he has not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that he has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has not submitted persuasive evidence that is sufficient to meet his “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. at 464. Therefore, we do not find that he has met his burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David L Neal
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 8787207 (BIA)

2017 WL 8787246 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCIO JUNIOR CUCCOLO A.K.A. MARCIO JUNIOR BATISTA-IZAIAS A.K.A. MARCIO B. IZAIAS
File: AXXX XX5 684 - Newark, NJ
December 11, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mikhail Usher, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 This case is before us pursuant to a February 24, 2016, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit granting the government’s unopposed motion to remand the record for further consideration. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The government requested a remand to allow the Board to consider the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s continuance request and any other issues that we deemed appropriate. There have been many significant developments in the law surrounding the application of the categorical and modified categorical approach and the determination of whether or not a statute is divisible for purposes of determining whether a conviction is an aggravated felony. Therefore, we will address this issue in addition to the denial of the continuance.
 
We find the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent any further continuance in this case. Further, we conclude that the criminal statute at issue is divisible under current case law, and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondent’s conviction for possessing, distributing, manufacturing, dispensing a controlled dangerous drug (Oxycodone), in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) (2013) is an aggravated felony (IJ at 2-3). Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, in whose jurisdiction these proceedings arise, has identified two “routes” to determine when a state drug conviction constitutes an aggravated felony: the “illicit trafficking” route and the “hypothetical felony” route, both tracking the statutory language. Gerbier v. Holmes, 280 F.3d 297, 305 (3d Cir. 2002) (citing Matter of Davis, 20 I&N Dec. 536 (BIA 1992)). Although we previously found otherwise, under more recent case law, we now conclude that the respondent has not been convicted of an aggravated felony pursuant to the “““illicit trafficking” route,” because under New Jersey law, “distribution” and ““dispensing” include no remuneration requirement. See Chang-Cruz v. Attorney General of the United States, 659 Fed. Appx. 114 (3d Cir. Aug. 24, 2016) (unpublished) (citing N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-2) and Matter of G.R., 929 A.2d 602, 605 (2007)). The conviction continues to be an aggravated felony, however, under the “hypothetical felony” route.
 
*2 Under the “hypothetical felony” route, an aggravated felony is defined in pertinent part as the “illicit trafficking in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act [21 U.S.C. § 802]), including a drug trafficking crime (as defined in section 924(c) of title 18, United States Code.” Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act (emphasis added). 18 U.S.C. § 924(c)(2) defines a “drug trafficking crime” as “any felony punishable under the Controlled Substances Act” (CSA). The United States Supreme Court has held that an offense (whether labeled a misdemeanor or a felony by the relevant jurisdiction) constitutes a drug trafficking crime if it “proscribes conduct punishable as a felony” under the CSA. Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. 1678, 1683 (2013) (quoting Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47, 60 (2006)). Thus, an offense comes within the “hypothetical felony” route when it necessarily prohibits conduct that the CSA likewise prohibits and punishes it by more than 1 year of imprisonment. Id. at 1683 (citing 18 U.S.C. § 3559(a)(5)).
 
In determining whether a state offense is a hypothetical federal felony, we apply the “categorical approach,” where we “focus solely on whether the elements of the crime of conviction sufficiently match the elements of [the] generic [federal offense], while ignoring the particular facts of the case.” Mathis v. United States, 136 S. Ct. 2243, 2248 (2016). This entails looking to whether the elements of the state statute of conviction categorically fit within the “generic” federal crime. Id. It is required to consider only the respective elements of the state and federal offenses to see whether a conviction under the state statute “‘necessarily’ involved facts equating to the generic federal offense.” Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. at 1684 (quoting Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13, 24 (2005) (plurality opinion). We do not look to the facts of the particular case, but rather look to see “whether the state statute defining the crime of conviction categorically fits within the generic federal definition of a corresponding aggravated felony.” See Singh v. Attorney General of the United States, 839 F.3d 273, 278 (3d Cir. 2016) (citing Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. at 1684). Thus, under the categorical approach, the respondent’s state drug conviction is an aggravated felony if the conduct necessary to sustain a state conviction includes all of the elements of an offense that could be convicted as a felony under the CSA (21 U.S.C. § 801 et seq.). See Gerbier v. Holmes, 280 F.3d at 803.
 
*3 The Immigration Judge may depart from the categorical approach to determine whether the statute of conviction is an aggravated felony if the statute is divisible. A statute is divisible if (a) it lists multiple discrete offenses as enumerated alternatives or defines a single offense by reference to disjunctive sets of “elements,” more than one combination of which could support a conviction; and (b) some (but not all) of those listed offenses or combinations of disjunctive elements are a categorical match to the relevant generic standard. Descamps v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 2276, 2283 (2013); see also United States v. Brown, 765 F.3d 278, 295 (3d Cir. 2014). Further, any facts that increase the prescribed range of penalties to which a criminal defendant is exposed are elements of the crime, and must be found beyond a reasonable doubt. United States v. Abbott, 748 F.3d 154, 159 (3d Cir. 2014) (citing Alleyne v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 2151, 2160 (2013) (quoting Apprendi v. New Jersey, 530 U.S. 466, 490 (2000)). In a case in which the type of controlled substance is not specified in the statute of conviction, but the type of controlled substance impacts the sentencing determination, the type of controlled substance is an element of the offense. Id. at 158-59.
 
At the time the respondent committed the offense, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) provided that it shall be unlawful for any person knowingly or purposely to manufacture, distribute or dispense, or to possess or have under his control with intent to manufacture, distribute or dispense, a controlled dangerous substance or controlled substance analog. The analogous federal statute under the CSA is 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1). Wilson v. Ashcroft, 350 F.3d 377, 381 (3d Cir. 2003).
 
Although the respondent argues that the statute is not divisible under Descamps, we disagree. The statute here is divisible because the type of controlled substance involved must be proven beyond a reasonable doubt by a jury. The jury instructions for N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) make it clear that the jury must find (a) the conduct involved; (b) the type of controlled substance involved; and (c) that the individual acted knowingly and purposefully. See N.J. J.I. Crim. § 2C:35-5. Given that more than one combination of elements could support a conviction and can impact the range of penalties, the statute is divisible, warranting application of the modified categorical approach. See Evanson v. Attorney General of U.S., 550 F.3d 284, 293 (3d Cir. 2008).
 
*4 A review of the documents in the respondent’s record of conviction establishes that the respondent pled guilty to indictment number 13-08-01039-A (Exh. 2). This indictment and the criminal accusation charge the respondent with possessing, distributing, manufacturing, dispensing a controlled dangerous drug (Oxycodone), in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) (Exhs. 2A and 2B). Oxycodone is a Schedule II controlled substance, 21 U.S.C. § 812(c); 21 C.F.R. § 1308.12(b)(1)(xiii), and the distribution of it is punishable by a term of imprisonment of more than 1 year. See 21 U.S.C. § 841(b). As the respondent’s offense of conviction is analogous to 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1), the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony for purposes of removability.2
 
We next consider the Immigration Judge’s denial of a further continuance. The respondent argues that he should have been provided more time to retain counsel with experience in both immigration and criminal law, in part, because he was pursuing post-conviction relief (Respondent’s Br. at 14-16, citing Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010)).3 He also argues that he was misinformed by two previous attorneys, but he has made no effort to substantially comply with the procedural requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Fadiga v. Attorney General of the U.S., 488 F.3d 142, 155 (3d Cir. 2007); Matter of Compean, Bangley & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). As such, there is no basis to remand the record as the respondent requests (Respondent’s Br. at 16).
 
The respondent was served with his Notice to Appear on December 1, 2014, and first appeared before an Immigration Judge on December 24, 2014 (Tr. at 1; Exh. 1). He was given a continuance to obtain representation (Tr. at 2). One month later, on January 22, 2015, he appeared without an attorney and was granted a continuance, but was informed that he would be required to go forward without an attorney should he not have one at the next hearing (Tr. at 4). On March 10, 2015, he again requested more time to obtain representation (Tr. at 6-12). The respondent was granted additional time to prepare his application for asylum and again appeared without an attorney on March 26, 2015 (Tr. at 17-18). At this hearing, he first indicated he no longer wished to apply for asylum, but changed his mind when he learned there was no other relief from removal available to him (Tr. at 20-23). He was then granted additional time to prepare his application, but at the final hearing, on April 16, 2015, again without representation, he stated that he would not be applying for asylum (Tr. at 29). Subsequently, the Immigration Judge entered his decision.
 
*5 The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal to the Board unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); see also Melgar v. Attorney Gen. of U.S., 442 Fed. App’x 695, 699 (3d Cir. 2011). Here, the respondent was provided ample time to obtain representation and the Immigration Judge properly denied the continuance. Also, as discussed above, the respondent has not shown he is eligible for any relief from removal. The respondent has not demonstrated that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case or that substantial prejudice resulted. See Fadiga v. Attorney General of the U.S., 488 F.3d at 155; Khan v. Attorney General, 448 F.3d 226, 236 (3d Cir. 2006).
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent, a native and citizen of Brazil, conceded that he was removable under section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B), for remaining in the United States longer than permitted by law (Exh. 1; Respondent’s Br. at 2, 14, 25).

2

The respondent is also removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act for a violation relating to a controlled substance (Exhs. 1, 2, 2A, and 2B).

3

Any attempt by the Respondent to collaterally attack his conviction would not affect present removability. Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993); see generally Matter of Khalik, 17 I&N Dec. 518 (BIA 1980). We further observe that the Immigration Judge’s order was entered on April 16, 2015, and there is no indication in the record that any challenge to his conviction has been successful.

2017 WL 8785840 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FAFA TOURAY
File: AXXX XX9 511 - Seattle, WA
December 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen C. Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret LaDow
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 13, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order entered on February 6, 2016. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)(2017). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The basis of the motion, which was filed on May 4, 2017, is that the respondent’s prior counsel neglected to remind him of the date and time of the February 6, 2016, hearing. The respondent argues that this amounts to ineffective assistance of counsel and thus “exceptional circumstances” excusing his absence from the February 6, 2016, hearing.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (IJ at 3-4). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597 (9th Cir. 2004) (discussing the circumstances under which compliance with these procedural requirements may be excused). In particular, the respondent’s supporting documents do not provide sufficient detail of the agreement with prior counsel, including the extent of prior counsel’s representation. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639 (providing that a party claiming ineffective assistance of counsel should provide “an affidavit . . . that sets forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken . . . and what counsel did or did not represent to the respondent in this regard”). While the supporting affidavit and text messages clearly reflect that the respondent entered into an agreement with prior counsel concerning the underlying visa petition, it is unclear if the agreement encompassed representation in these removal proceedings (IJ at 3; cf. Aff. at ¶8).1 That prior counsel retained copies of the respondent’s immigration records, including a copy of the underlying hearing notice, does not persuade us of an agreement by prior counsel to represent the respondent in these removal proceedings (cf. Respondent’s Br. at 10-11). For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent has not properly raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.2
 
*2 Even assuming the respondent has properly raised this claim, we further agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion is untimely (IJ at 2). A motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal on the basis of exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the date of entry the order. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. While this period may be equitably tolled as the result of ineffective assistance of counsel, the tolling period is not indefinite. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011). Rather, once an alien “definitively learns of the harm” resulting from the complained instance of deficient performance or “obtains “‘vital information bearing on the existence of his claim”DD’, the tolling period ends and the statutory deadline begins to run. Id.; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The Immigration Judge concluded that the tolling period ended on February 15, 2016 (IJ at 2). Contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal (Respondent’s Br. at 5-6), we agree with the Immigration Judge that any tolling ended on this date. As noted in the affidavit accompanying the motion, this is the date that the respondent was advised by prior counsel that he had missed the hearing and would need to file a motion to reopen (IJ at 2; Aff. at ¶11). The respondent “““definitively learn[ed] of the harm” resulting from the complained instance of deficient performance--prior counsel’s failure to remind him of the hearing date--upon receiving this information (IJ at 2). Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d at 679. Indeed, as the respondent concedes on appeal, upon receiving this information from prior counsel, the “[r]espondent became aware of potential grave consequences of missing his hearing” (Respondent’s Br. at 6). Thus, consultation with current counsel and current counsel’s review of the respondent’s file was not necessary to definitively learn of the harm caused by the alleged misconduct of prior counsel (see id.). Cf. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 895.
 
As the Immigration Judge properly determined, with the tolling period ending on February 15, 2016, the 180-day deadline extended to August 15, 2016 (IJ at 2). The May 4, 2017, motion is therefore untimely.
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that the circumstances surrounding his failure to appear amount to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of the Act. “The term “‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act (emphasis added); see also Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996). The “particularized facts presented in each case” are relevant to determining whether the respondent has established “exceptional circumstances,” and the “plain language of the statute indicates that this is a difficult burden to meet.” Arredondo v. Lynch, 824 F.3d 801, 806 (9th Cir. 2016) (internal quotation marks and citations omitted).
 
*3 As an initial matter, we emphasize that this is not a case where the respondent was misinformed by his attorney, either that there was no hearing or that the hearing was on an incorrect date. Cf Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 896 (9th Cir. 2003), as amended, 339 F.3d 1012 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 474 (BIA 1996). Nor is this a case where the respondent was uninformed of a hearing date after notice was provided to his attorney. To the contrary, the respondent admits receiving the Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings advising him of the February 6, 2016, hearing before the Seattle Immigration Court (Exh. 2; Aff. at ¶¶7-8). The correct hearing date and time were therefore conveyed to the respondent, and this information was not contradicted by any actions of prior counsel.
 
While we recognize that the respondent eventually gave the original copy of the hearing notice to prior counsel (Aff. at ¶¶7-10), the respondent’s later confusion about the hearing date and his own lack of record keeping were not beyond his control (IJ at 4). Furthermore, despite the respondent’s claim that prior counsel failed to respond to his inquiries regarding the hearing status, the respondent always had the option, which he never exercised, of contacting the Immigration Court to learn the details of this hearing (Id. at 3).3 Considering the record in its entirety, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances within the meaning of section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
For these reasons, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach).
 
In conclusion, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to not exercise his sua sponte reopening authority in this case (IJ at 4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent potentially becoming eligible for additional relief after the period for timely reopening expired does establish exceptional circumstances so as to provide a basis for granting reopening sua sponte (cf. Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

There is no record of prior counsel entering an appearance in these proceedings.

2

We do not share in the Immigration Judge’s concerns regarding the lack of notice to prior counsel and the lack of a complaint to appropriate disciplinary authorities (IJ at 3-4), in light of the evidence presented that prior counsel has been suspended from the practice of law (Mot. at 58). See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 896-97 (9th Cir. 2008) (excusing compliance with these requirements as futile under similar circumstances).

3

Additionally, while we recognize the respondent’s contention that he did not know his alien identification number (A-number) (Aff. at ¶10), there is is a considerable difference between being denied access to information for not providing an A-number and not making any attempt to contact the Immigration Court.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO AGUILAR-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX0 019 - San Diego, CA
December 1, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James B. Rudolph, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amelia C. Anderson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 29, 2014, which denied his motion to reopen removal proceedings. Pursuant to the Board’s October 11, 2016, remand, the Immigration Judge issued another decision on June 22, 2017,1 further specifying the reasons for denying the motion to reopen. Thereafter, the parties provided arguments on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record shows that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) placed the respondent in removal proceedings with personal service of the Notice to Appear on August 12, 2013, alleging that he was a native and citizen of Mexico and charging him as inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (2012) (present without being admitted or paroled) (Exh. 1). The record indicates that the respondent, who was represented by counsel, admitted the allegations and conceded the charge. On May 28, 2014, the respondent applied solely for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, which the Immigration Judge granted with an alternate order of removal to Mexico.
 
The respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen on September 30, 2014, alleging ineffective assistance from former counsel. The DHS opposed the motion. In the October 29, 2014, decision, the Immigration Judge adopted the reasoning from the DHS opposition and denied the motion to reopen without further reasoning.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s June 22, 2017, denial of the motion to reopen. The motion is untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent concedes that the motion is untimely but contends that the 90-day filing requirement should be equitably tolled on account of ineffective assistance from former counsel. However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), because he has not filed a complaint concerning former counsel with the State bar or reasonably explained why.
 
*2 In this regard, the respondent argues that filing a bar complaint is not required because former counsel has been substantially compliant because he has responded to the letter from current counsel and because he provided a reason for not filing a motion to suppress. Respondent’s Br. at 6-7. However, in the context of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, “substantial compliance” with the Lozada requirements generally refers to substantial compliance by the complaining party, the respondent, not the attorney whose competence is being challenged.
 
In addition, to date, the respondent has not substantiated at all the allegation that he was the subject of racial or ethnic profiling by local police, which is the underlying basis for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Thus, there is no sufficient basis to absolve the respondent from filing a State bar complaint against his former attorney or, by extension, to equitably toll the filing deadline for the motion to reopen.
 
We note that the respondent states in the brief on appeal that former counsel was suspended from the practice of law on November 13, 2014, and on December 21, 2015, and was disbarred on August 14, 2016. Respondent’s Br. at 7. However, those disciplinary actions do not relieve the respondent of complying with the bar complaint requirement when he filed his motion to reopen on September 30, 2014. See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 897 (9th Cir. 2009).
 
In conclusion, we agree that the motion to reopen is untimely and that the respondent has not shown that the 90-day filing requirement should be equitably tolled. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the decision is dated June 22, 2016, which appears to be an administrative or clerical error. The decision was served by mail on the parties on June 23, 2017.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MELVIN ANTONIO DIAZ-CASTRO
File: AXXX XX0 462 - Houston, TX
November 28, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Randy Alexander, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on November 30, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s January 27, 2016, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the order of removal entered in absentia on May 2, 2006. On April 24, 2017, the respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador filed the present request for reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent does not dispute that the present motion exceeds the time limits applicable to motions to reopen. Rather, he contends that such limits should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance from former counsel. The respondent asserts that, but for former counsel’s deficient performance, he would have succeeded on his asserted lack of proper notice claim on appeal before the Board.
 
However, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing a claim against former counsel, including the requirement that he show prejudice resulting from former counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (noting that to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that counsel’s performance resulted in substantial prejudice); Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (proving prejudice requires the petitioner to make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief the sought).
 
It is undisputed that the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, which included the date, time, and place of his scheduled hearing and was read to him in Spanish. As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent’s claimed inability to understand that he was required to appear in court as directed does not defeat the fact that the respondent was personally served with and received proper notice of the date, time, and place of his May 2006 scheduled hearing (I.J. at 2), which was not reversed by the Board.
 
Moreover, the respondent has proffered no argument or evidence addressing the full breadth of both the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s reasoning, namely that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in waiting more than 9 years to file a motion to reopen and rescind the May 2006 in absentia removal order. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated that, absent counsel’s alleged deficient performance, he would have succeeded on appeal before the Board. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d at 85.
 
*2 Moreover, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv), the respondent does not need to have his removal proceedings reopened to be able to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). We also note that the respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) while any waiver or provisional waiver application is pending before the USCIS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a).
 
The respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of the present motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reconsideration of the prior Board decision or reopening of these proceedings in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. See, e.g., Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s sua sponte power is limited to exceptional circumstances). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 8785875 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 8785885 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADEILSON MARTINS-SOARES
File: AXXX XX3 508 - Harlingen, TX
November 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Becki A. Jacobson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Luciana Dubuc
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Brazil, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 24, 2017, which denied his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on July 1, 2005. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a motion for summary affirmance in opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed (i) within 180 days after the date of the removal order if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (ii) at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
On appeal, the respondent abandons his lack of notice claim. He admits that he received notice of his hearing on July 1, 2005, but he did not appear because he was afraid that he would be returned to Brazil (Respondent’s Br. at 7). On appeal, the respondent also filed a motion to remand in order to apply for asylum based on changed country conditions (Respondent’s Br. at 20-21). The respondent argues that his prior counsel provided him ineffective assistance when he failed to investigate and explore other potential applications for relief (Respondent’s Br. at 8, 15-19).
 
The respondent conceded that he received notice of his hearing on July 1, 2005, that he did not appear at the hearing to avoid removal, and that his motion to reopen was untimely (Respondent’s Appeal; Respondent’s Declaration). We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received effective notice of the July 1, 2005, hearing. It follows that the respondent’s motion did not meet the statutory grounds for rescission of his order of removal under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 An alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order need not first rescind the order before seeking reopening of the proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered. Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013). Although the Immigration Judge’s order did not specifically address this issue, we discern no basis for a remand, inasmuch as the respondent’s motion did not establish changed country conditions in Brazil arising after the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s decision on July 1, 2005. The respondent’s declaration indicates that he owed an unscrupulous lender a large sum of money in 2005 and that he paid off the debt he owed (Respondent’s Declaration at 3). In his declaration, he also asserted that he fears of general violence and corruption in Brazil based on reports from friends (Respondent’s Declaration at 4-5). The respondent did not provide any country conditions reports from which to compare the country conditions in Brazil in 2005 and the country conditions in 2017. Therefore, the respondent did not present prima facie evidence of changed country conditions arising in the respondent’s country of nationality or country to which removal was ordered. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 981 (BIA 1997); see also Singh v. Lynch, 840 F.3d 220, 222-23 (5th Cir. 2016) (new threats and violence experienced by the alien’s mother amounted to a change in personal circumstances); Ramos-Lopez v. Lynch, 823 F.3d 1024, 1026 (5th Cir. 2016) (noting that the alien’s briefing does not compare in any meaningful way the conditions in her home country in 1998 and 2013, therefore, she did not present material evidence of changed country conditions).
 
The respondent argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by filing the motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal without reviewing the respondent’s immigration file, preparing affidavits, or submitting evidence supporting the motion (Respondent’s Br. at 15-19). Based on his prior attorney’s ineffective performance, the respondent requests remand and reopening in order to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. We assume that the respondent met the procedural requirements of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board and requiring a showing of prejudice). However, as discussed above, the respondent did not establish that he was prejudiced because he did not establish a prima facie case showing that changed country conditions arising in Brazil since 2005 have caused him to fear future harm or persecution in Brazil in 2017. Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006) (finding substantial prejudice is required to show ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 40.
 
*3 The respondent indicates that he is the beneficiary of an approved Petition for Alien Relative, Form I-130, filed on his behalf by his United States citizen spouse. We note that effective August 29, 2016, new rules regarding provisional unlawful presence waivers were implemented. See Expansion of Provisional Unlawful Presence Waivers of Inadmissibility, 81 Fed. Reg. 50,244, 50,255-56, 50,262 (July 29, 2016). The DHS has amended the rule to allow individuals with final removal orders to apply for provisional unlawful presence waivers if they have filed an application seeking consent to reapply for admission and such an application has been conditionally approved by the USCIS. See 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv). Thus, the respondent does not need to have his removal proceedings reopened to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver with the USCIS. The respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS while any waiver or provisional waiver applications are pending before the USCIS. 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 8785885 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANGELINA DIEGO BASILIO MAGDALENA EVELIN DIEGO
Files: AXXX XX1 523 - San Diego, CA
AXXX XX1 524
November 21, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
David Landry, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of Guatemala, filed a timely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 2017, decision ordering them removed from the United States to Guatemala. The respondents also filed a motion to remand the proceedings to the Immigration Court to allow the lead respondent (hereinafter “the respondent”)1 an opportunity to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that prior counsel provided her with ineffective assistance by not seeking asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture on her behalf. The respondent asserts that former counsel did not inform her of the possibility of applying for these forms of relief although she told him she was afraid to return to Guatemala, and he misrepresented to the Immigration Judge that they had discussed the possibility of such relief (Tr. at 20, 25-26). The respondent’s motion is accompanied by a completed Form I-589, Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal; a State Bar of California Attorney Complaint Form, a copy of which was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel; and declarations from the respondent, the respondent’s husband, and the Spanish/English translator employed by current counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 537 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
The Board has limited ability to engage in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). The determination whether the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel and, if so, whether she was prejudiced involves findings of fact. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (stating that alien must show prejudice in order to prevail on ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Therefore, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

The respondents are a mother (AXXX XXX XXX) and her minor daughter (AXXX XXX XXX).

2017 WL 8785890 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA MARGARITA AREBALO-DIAZ
File: AXXX XX5 937 - Harlingen, TX
November 20, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Divyesh R. Gopal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christine H. Kim
Law Clerk
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On April 7, 2017, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind a January 10, 2003, in absentia order of removal. The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, now appeals. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility and what is likely to happen to the respondent, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In general, an alien may file one motion to reopen, and any such motion must be filed within 90 days of the entry of the final administrative order of removal. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) (2012); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The Act and regulations provide an exception to the general rule in the case of an alien who seeks to rescind an in absentia order of removal. An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded only by a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances, or a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that she did not receive notice in accordance with section 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act. See section 240(c)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The Board and the Immigration Courts also have reopened cases filed more than 90 days after a final administrative order in which an alien proves that his claim was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel and when the motion was filed within a reasonable time after discovering the ineffective assistance. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel is governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion because the respondent did not demonstrate that she did not receive notice of her January 10, 2003, hearing. The record reflects that the Notice to Appear, which was personally served on the respondent on August 13, 2002, indicated that the respondent’s next hearing was scheduled for January 10, 2003, in Harlingen, Texas (IJ at 1). The Certificate of Service bears the respondent’s signature and indicates that she was provided oral notice in Spanish of the consequences of her failure to appear (IJ at 1; Exh. 1).
 
*2 The respondent concedes that the Notice to Appear contained the time, date, and location of the hearing, but contends that she did not understand the consequences of her failure to appear due to the immigration officer’s poor Spanish translation (IJ at 1; Respondent’s Br. at 3, 14-15, 17, 20, 21; Motion at 8). We are not persuaded by this argument. In her supporting affidavit the respondent indicated that she sought the help of Cejas Enterprise, INC, to file a change of address form because she did not understand English (IJ at 2; Motion to Reopen, Tab F). The warning regarding the change of address requirement is in the paragraph entitled “Failure to appear,” which also informs the alien that a removal order may be issued if the alien fails “to attend the hearing at the time and place designated on this notice.” Given that the warnings are in the same paragraph, we are not persuaded that the respondent understood she had to file a change of address form but did not understand the consequences of her failure to appear (Respondent’s Br. at 20). Additionally, the respondent’s argument that she was not informed of the right to a change of venue has no bearing on whether she received notice of her January 10, 2003, hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 3, 16; Motion at 9).
 
We also affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that equitable tolling of the filing deadline was not warranted in the respondent’s case because the respondent did not demonstrate reasonable diligence (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 22). In Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, recognized that under its equitable tolling standard “a litigant is entitled to equitable tolling of a statute of limitations only if the litigant establishes two elements: ‘(1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.”DD’ Id. at 344. See also Holland v. Florida, 560 U.S. 631, 649 (2010) (citing Pace v. DiGuglielmo, 544 U.S. 408, 418 (2005)). Taking this case law into consideration, we conclude that equitable tolling is not warranted in the respondent’s case because she did not demonstrate that she pursued her rights with “reasonable diligence.” Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d at 344. The respondent acknowledged that she did not seek legal advice until 2012, when she and her husband retained their former counsel to file a motion to reopen and a Form I-130 (Petition for Alien Relative) based on their 2012 marriage (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 4-5; Motion at 4, Tab F).
 
*3 The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge violated her due process rights because she did not consider the totality of the circumstances when she denied the motion to reopen for lack of exceptional circumstances (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 21-22, 24-25). The respondent states that her life in the years after her in absentia order of removal was dedicated to taking care of her husband, who had health issues, and her children (Respondent’s Br. at 21; Motion at 14). She also claims that her former counsel’s failure to file a motion to reopen in 2012 amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel and added to her immigration dilemma (Respondent’s Br. at 25).
 
Our review of the record does not reflect that the Immigration Judge violated the respondent’s due process rights. See Bouchikhi v. Holder, 676 F.3d 173, 180 (5th Cir. 2012) (explaining that to prevail on a due process violation claim the respondent must show substantial prejudice); see also Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356 (BIA 1983). The respondent did not explain how her husband’s health issues prevented her from attending her January 10, 2003, hearing.1 Her desire not to be separated from her husband and children does not excuse her failure to appear, and does not amount to an exceptional circumstance (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Motion at 14). Further, to extent the respondent relies on her former counsel’s ineffective assistance of counsel in failing to file a motion to reopen in 2012, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the failure to file a motion in 2012 has no bearing on why the respondent failed to appear at her January 10, 2003, hearing (IJ at 2 n1; Respondent’s Br. at 7, 23, 25; Motion at 13). Finally, as stated previously, a motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. The respondent filed her motion on March 20, 2017, more than 14 years after the in absentia order of removal (IJ at 2). Considering the totality of the circumstances, we are not persuaded that the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen violated the respondent’s due process rights.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The medical records provided with the respondent’s motion to reopen are from 2009. Thus, it is unclear how the husband’s health issues impacted the respondent’s failure to appear at her January 10, 2003, hearing.

IN RE: ANTONIO SILVA ALVES

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kerry E. Doyle, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on January 25, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his applications adjustment of status and voluntary departure in the exercise of discretion. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen due to the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, the respondent has established that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s deficient performance including not calling the respondent to testify and not providing more evidence in support of his applications for relief. Further, the respondent’s sole conviction for operating under the influence of intoxicating liquor and failing to stop for police, while very serious, occurred 9 years ago. In light of the evidence presented, the respondent positive equities, and the non-opposition by the DHS, we will grant the motion and remand the proceedings to provide the respondent a further opportunity to pursue relief. We do not express an opinion on the merits of the respondent’s entitlement to relief. On remand, the Immigration Judge may receive any additional evidence he deems appropriate to the full resolution of this matter. Accordingly, the motion will be granted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with this order.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

2017 WL 7660464 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALAMATOU ISSAKA MAIKIDO
File: AXXX XX5 890 - Charlotte, NC
October 25, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter E. Torres, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cori White
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Niger, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 20, 2017, which denied her motion to reopen removal proceedings. The parties have provided arguments on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record shows that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) personally served the respondent with a Notice to Appear on August 23, 2013 (Exh. 1). During these proceedings, the respondent was represented by counsel, S. Wayne Patterson. On February 6, 2014, the Immigration Judge granted a period of pre-conclusion voluntary departure until June 6, 2014, with an alternate order of removal.
 
On May 6, 2014, the respondent, through new counsel, Ann Marie Dooley, filed a motion to reopen to terminate her voluntary departure and secure a final order of removal. The Immigration Judge issued an order in response on May 9, 2014.
 
On June 2, 2017, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen in order to pursue an application for for asylum and withholding of removal under sections 208 and 241(b)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158, 1231(b)(3) (2012), and protection under the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16(c). The motion alleged ineffective assistance from attorney Patterson. The Immigration Judge denied the motion in the June 20, 2017, decision on appeal.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. As the Immigration Judge stated, the motion is number-barred, and it is time-barred as well. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent has not shown or alleged changed country conditions in Niger to demonstrate that her motion should be excepted from the time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Rather, she contends that the motion is not subject to the time and number limits, or should be granted sua sponte, because of ineffective assistance she received from attorney Patterson. The respondent asserts that the claimed ineffective assistance took two forms: (1) the filing of a deficient application for prosecutorial discretion; and (2) the failure to file an application for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab A at 2, Tab B at 9-10). Respondent’s Br. at 16-17. The respondent’s contentions are not persuasive.
 
*2 Prosecutorial discretion is a matter that is wholly within the jurisdiction of the DHS. Matter of W-Y-U-, 27 I&N Dec. 17, 19 (BIA 2017); Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 694 (BIA 2012). Thus, even if the application for prosecutorial discretion were deficient, the Board’s authority to protect a respondent’s due process rights does not extend beyond the scope of our jurisdiction. Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 339 (BIA 1991).
 
As for an asylum application, the application submitted with the motion is predicated on a fear of her husband’s family in Niger subjecting herself or her daughters to female genital mutilation (FGM) (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B at Exh. 16). However, the claim as is relates to the children is foreclosed by precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit. See Niang v. Gonzales, 492 F.3d 505 (4th Cir. 2007). Thus, any failure to pursue asylum on this basis is not prejudicial to the respondent. See Ilunga v. Holder, 777 F.3d 199, 208 (4th Cir. 2015) (establishing a due process violation requires a showing that the violation caused prejudice such that the result of the proceedings were likely impacted). As for the claim as it relates to the respondent herself, we do not find that the statements provided by the respondent establish that the terms of attorney Patterson’s representation of the respondent clearly included the filing of an asylum application (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab B, Exh. 1 at 15, Exh. 9 at 4). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge that this case does not present exceptional circumstances that warrant reopening sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The arguments on appeal in this regard, which rely in large part on the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel, do not persuade us to conclude otherwise. Respondent’s Br. at 19-21. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARK SEMANA GAVIOLA
File: AXXX XX8 351 - New York, NY
October 24, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Salvador E. Tuy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on November 8, 2012, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2010, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has now filed a motion with the Board to reopen and rescind his in absentia removal order based on the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The respondent essentially contends that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance by (a) advising the respondent not to attend the hearing on November 8, 2010, based on the anticipated grant of a continuance, and (b) not acknowledging this erroneous advice in subsequent filings (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2, 4, 6, Exh. A). The record before us does not contain a brief in opposition from the Department of Homeland Security. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reopen is both time and number barred. With certain exceptions not pertinent here, an alien is entitled to file one motion to reopen (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative decision. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). This is the respondent’s second motion to reopen and he did not file this motion to reopen until March 30, 2017, well past the 90-day filing deadline.
 
The respondent initially argues that he filed his motion to reopen and rescind in a timely manner given the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, which qualifies as “exceptional circumstances.” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 1, 7-10). We disagree. Even assuming the alleged ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former counsel qualifies as “exceptional circumstances” under the Act, the respondent’s motion to reopen is still untimely as it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia order issued by the Immigration Judge on November 8, 2010. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.2(c)(3), 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent also has not submitted evidence demonstrating that he did not receive proper notice of the hearing on November 8, 2010, under sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1) or (2); and we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s unsupported contention that the erroneous advice of his former counsel is equivalent to a lack of notice under the Act (see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 9-10).
 
*2 We also conclude that the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen does not qualify for equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of former counsel. In order to warrant equitable tolling of the applicable time limitation based on ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien is required to demonstrate “due diligence” in pursuing his claim during “both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131 (2d Cir. 2008); see also Celtic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006).
 
Here, the respondent has not meaningfully explained how he acted with due diligence prior to retaining current counsel and filing the instant motion to reopen on March 20, 2017. As set out above, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal on December 21, 2010, and the Board subsequently dismissed the appeal from this decision on November 8, 2012. The respondent alleges that he was unaware of his former counsel’s ineffective assistance until 2016, when his former counsel was disbarred and the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit affirmed the Board’s decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 8-9, 11, 16, Exh. A at 2, Exhs. B, H). However, an alien is required to exercise due diligence during the entire period he seeks to equitably toll. Consequently, the respondent does not qualify for equitable tolling because he did not take steps to preserve his rights after the Immigration Judge’s adverse decision in 2010, or even after the Board’s adverse decision in 2012. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F3d at 133 (finding that due diligence required alien to follow up with his attorney after adverse decision); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, (2d Cir. 2000) (finding that alien did not exercise due diligence after adverse Board decision where he attempted to reach his attorney and then did nothing for 2 years).1
 
The respondent also requests that we sua sponte reopen the proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) so he may pursue an application for cancellation of removal. Based on the circumstances presented in this case, we do not find that the respondent has shown “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. A-N). See Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to sua sponte reopen proceedings is reserved for “exceptional situations” and “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.”).2 To the extent the respondent is seeking reopening based on the potential hardship to his family (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 17), we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Aside from the lack of due diligence required for equitable tolling, the respondent has also not substantially complied with the procedural requirements for bringing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (holding that an “alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements . . . forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim.”). In particular, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with his former counsel or evidence that he informed his former counsel of the allegations against him and provided him with an opportunity to respond to the allegations. Even though the respondent allegedly attempted to contact his former attorney via phone (Motion to Reopen, Exh. A), the respondent has not explained why he did not attempt to inform his former counsel of the allegations via some other means, like traditional or electronic mail.

2

Although not dispositive to our decision, we reject the respondent’s contention that his grounds for removal no longer exist because his 1995 California conviction for assault with great bodily injury was set aside and dismissed in 2008 pursuant to section 1203.4 of the California Penal Code (Motion to Reopen at 16, Exh. I). Section 1203.4 of the California Penal Code is a rehabilitative statute that does not remove the immigration consequences of a conviction and sentence. See Matter of De Millan, 26 I&N Dec. 904, 908 (BIA 2017) (stating that section 1203.4 of the California Penal Code is a rehabilitative statute with a number of limitations on relief even under California law, including not affecting the existence of a conviction for the purpose of any subsequent criminal proceedings); Matter of Marroquin-Garcia, 23 I&N Dec. 705, 714-17 (A.G. 2005) (determining that the expungement or vacatur of a judgment pursuant to section 1203.4 of the California Penal Code, a rehabilitative statute, is still deemed a “conviction” for immigration purposes under the Act).

2017 WL 6555159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL LOPEZ-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX4 513 - Miami, FL
October 12, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack E. Wallace, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on March 29, 2017, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider this decision.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
Cast as a motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion fails to indicate any error in law or fact which would persuade this Board to reconsider our prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). In his motion, the respondent argues that the Board erred in its assessment of his prior attorney’s admission of alienage, and argues that because his prior attorney made a mistake in admitting alienage, it cannot be considered tactical and the respondent’s resulting removal order is unjust. See Santiago-Rodriguez v. Holder, 657 F.3d 820, 831-32 (9th Cir. 2011). However, we continue to note that the respondent does not allege that his admission of alienage is untrue or otherwise inaccurate, and we find no reason to disturb our previous finding that his assertion of prejudice, under Matter of Lozada, 18 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), is speculative in nature.
 
With his motion, the respondent has submitted a letter from Thomas A. Kroeger, Counsel for the Florida Bar, indicating that “it cannot be said that (prior counsel’s) conduct demonstrated a lack of competence or diligence,” and noting that its standards differ from ineffective assistance of counsel claims in criminal or immigration proceedings. Cast as a timely motion to reopen proceedings, we do not find this letter material to these proceedings or likely to change the result of the respondent’s case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992). As such, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

It appears that the respondent currently has pending litigation before the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit. The parties should alert the Eleventh Circuit of the issuance of this decision.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BHAVESH KUMAR NATAVARBHAI PATEL
File: AXXX XX6 046 - Harlingen, TX
October 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bhavya Chaudhary, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maribel Mercado
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 26, 2017, denying his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has also requested a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed, and the stay request will be denied as moot.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent has not established that reopening is warranted on account of lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The record establishes that the Immigration Court properly mailed the Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the respondent’s March 22, 2012, removal hearing, to his then-attorney of record (NOH dated February 2, 2012). While the respondent’s counsel argues that Attorney Castillo was not the respondent’s attorney, statements by counsel are not evidence and thus are not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). The respondent’s counsel’s statements are not supported by a personal affidavit from the respondent indicating the same.
 
Moreover, a review of the record reflects that three attorneys from the same law firm, including Attorney Castillo, submitted Notices of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Forms EOIR-28), on behalf of the respondent in the proceedings below. Notice sent to an alien’s attorney of record is considered constructive notice to the alien. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c), 1292.5; Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 258 (BIA 1985).
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that his failure to appear was due to ineffective assistance by his former attorney, such claim is untimely as it was not presented within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order, and he has not demonstrated due diligence in pursuing such claim. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Further, the respondent has not complied with the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent seeks sua sponte reopening of these proceedings, he has not demonstrated that an exceptional situation exists. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We recognize that the respondent has pending applications before the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). However, a final order of removal does not preclude the respondent from pursuing those applications with the USCIS. The respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS pending the adjudication of his applications for relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a); see also 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv) (amending the rules regarding provisional unlawful presence waivers to permit individuals with final removal orders to apply for provisional unlawful presence waivers in certain instances).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s stay request is denied as moot.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 6555142 (BIA)
